Baqillani's Dialectical Refutation of the Magians on Shaytan
Baqillani questions the Magians on shaytan's origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a dilemma to argue that the Magian dualistic position on shaytan leads to the same conclusion that God creates evil, thereby refuting their dualism.

Core Idea
Dualism cannot escape the conclusion that God creates evil.

Baqillani engages the Magians on their own ground, asking a simple question about shaytan. Through a series of forced choices, he reveals that whether they claim shaytan is eternal or originated, they end up contradicting their core beliefs. This dialogue demonstrates a classic method of refutation by dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure of the argument.
Baqillani uses very direct questions; imagine the debate.
2
Identify the key dilemma
Find the two options Baqillani presents about shaytan: eternal or originated. Trace the consequences of each.
The Magians cannot accept either without giving up their dualism.
3
Analyze the consequence chain
If originated, follow the further question about the originator. Baqillani forces them to admit God as originator.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The passage ends with the conclusion that God creates evil and is wise. This destroys the Magian claim that evil cannot come from God.
The phrase 'لا فصل في هذا' means there is no distinction between good and evil in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أخرى في نقض المجوس، وكذلك يسألون فيقال لهم : خبرونا عن الشيطان : أمحدّث هو عندكم أم قديم؟ فإن قالوا قديم تركوا قولهم وإن قالوا محدث، قيل لهم : أفمن محدث حدث أم لا من محدث؟ فإن قالوا : لا من محدث قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من وقوع الحوادث لا من محدث؟ وإن قالوا من محدث قيل لهم : ومَن محدثه؟ فإن قالوا الله سبحانه ولا بد من ذلك، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يُحدث الباري سبحانه سائر الشرور ويكون بذلك حكيماً غير سفيه؟ ولا فصل في هذا .
Another issue in refuting the Magians. And likewise they are asked, and it is said to them: 'Inform us about shaytan: is it originated (muhdath) in your view or eternal (qadim)?' If they say eternal, they abandon their statement. And if they say originated, it is said to them: 'Is it from an originator or not from an originator?' If they say: 'Not from an originator,' it is said to them: 'What prevents you from the occurrence of events without an originator?' And if they say: 'From an originator,' it is said to them: 'And who originated it?' If they say: 'God, exalted, and there is no alternative,' it is said to them: 'What prevents you from [admitting] that the Creator (al-Bari), exalted, creates all evils and thereby is wise, not foolish? There is no distinction in this.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian dualists. Baqillani forces them into a dilemma about shaytan: either it is eternal, which would contradict their belief in two eternal principles (since they likely only attribute eternity to light), or it is originated. If originated, then either it came from no originator (leading to no creator) or from an originator. If from an originator, that originator must be God. Consequently, God creates evils, which they deny. Thus, they cannot avoid the conclusion that God creates evil, or else they must accept the consequence of no originator.

Key Terms
المجوس (al-majus: Magians)
قديم (qadim: eternal)
محدث (muhdath: originated)
محدث (muhdith: originator)
الباري (al-Bari: the Creator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dualism (believing in two eternal principles of good and evil) avoids the problem of evil because evil is attributed to an independent power.
Correction: Baqillani shows that even in dualism, the devil (evil) must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, it conflicts with the dualist's own system. If originated, it must be from an originator, who is God, thus God is the creator of evil.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate consequence if the Magians say shaytan is eternal?
A) They admit shaytan is from an originator.
B) They abandon their own statement (contradiction).
C) They accept that God creates evil.
D) They deny the existence of evil.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dilemma forces the Magians to admit that God is the originator of shaytan and thus the creator of all evil, which they cannot differentiate from good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your own life, do you instinctively question God's wisdom or goodness? How does this lesson's claim that God creates all evil and remains wise challenge your immediate reaction? Will you commit to trusting His wisdom even when you cannot see the good?
?
What 'Shaytans' in your life—people, circumstances, or your own desires—do you tend to blame as independent sources of evil, separate from God's decree? Will you now acknowledge that every trial comes from Him and respond with patience and gratitude?
?
If dualism is false and God alone is the originator of all things, including evil, then what comfort or excuse do you have for your own sins? Can you still claim that 'the devil made me do it' or that external forces override your responsibility? Will you own your actions before God and seek His forgiveness?

Cross-Refuting Internal Disagreements: The Origin of Shaytan
Baqillani uses one group's claim to refute another's on the devil's origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the internal contradictions among various groups to refute their claims about the origin of the devil.

Core Idea
One group's claim refutes another's: internal disagreement invalidates erroneous theories.

Baqillani demonstrates a powerful refutation method: he does not need to disprove each heretical group individually. Instead, he shows that their own contradictory claims cancel each other out. This passage illustrates how the disagreements among those who speculate about the origin of shaytan actually serve as evidence against all of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the refutation structure.
2
Identify the four claims
The passage mentions four different proposed origins: persons of light, thought, doubt, and punishment.
3
Follow the refutation chain
Notice how each claim is used to counter the previous one, forming a chain of mutual refutations.
4
Understand the strategy
Recognize that Baqillani is not endorsing any of these views; he is exposing their inconsistency.
This is a common polemical technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعارض من قال : إن الشيطان حدث أشخاص النور بقول من قال : حدث عن فكرة ؛ ويعارض أهلُ الفكر بأصحاب الشَّكُ ؛ ويعارَضُ الفريقان بقول أصحاب العقاب.
And it is countered against the one who says that shaytan originated from the persons of light (ashkhas al-nur) by the statement of the one who says 'it originated from thought (or idea)'; and the adherents of thought are countered by the adherents of doubt; and the two groups are countered by the statement of the adherents of punishment.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This short passage presents a refutation strategy: Baqillani uses the claims of one group against another. Those who say shaytan originated from 'persons of light' (i.e., from the good principle) are refuted by those who say it originated from 'thought'. The latter group (adherents of thought) are refuted by those who say from 'doubt'. And both groups together are refuted by those who say from 'punishment'. Thus, their internal disagreements invalidate their positions, as each can be used against the other.

Key Terms
أشخاص النور (ashkhas al-nur: persons of light)
الفكرة (al-fikra: thought/idea)
الشك (al-shakk: doubt)
العقاب (al-uquba: punishment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These are separate valid theories about the origin of shaytan.
Correction: Baqillani does not present them as valid; he shows they contradict each other, thus refuting all of them.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the different groups regarding the origin of shaytan?
A) By proving each is false independently.
B) By using one group's claim to refute another's.
C) By appealing to scripture.
D) By denying the existence of shaytan.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes multiple heretical groups by using their own contradictory claims against each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreements among people who oppose the truth, do you feel confusion or confidence? This lesson shows that their contradictions expose their falsehood. Will you let this strengthen your trust in the truth you hold, or will you allow their noise to shake you?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief without checking if it contradicts other things you hold true? This passage warns that inconsistency is a sign of falsehood. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs for hidden contradictions, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
Baqillani uses the opponents' own words against them. Do you know the claims of falsehood well enough to refute them? Or have you avoided learning about opposing views? Commit today to learn at least one false argument so you can respond with truth.

Baqillani's Challenge to Magian Dualism
Baqillani challenges Magians on the eternity of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a logical parity argument to challenge the Magian belief in the eternity of both light and darkness.

Core Idea
If light is eternal, why not darkness?

In dealing with dualist sects like the Magians, kalam theologians often used parity arguments. Baqillani here confronts the Magian claim that light is eternal (and identified with God) while darkness is not. He challenges them to explain why darkness could not also be eternal if light can be. This passage shows a key method: forcing the opponent to justify their distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's rhetorical question sink in.
The passage is short but pregnant with argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
Ask yourself: What assumption does Baqillani grant the Magians? What consequence does he draw? Why is this question difficult for them?
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في نقض المجوس أيضاً ، ويقال لهم : إذا جاز قِدَم النور الذي هو الباري ، فما أنكرتم من قدم الشيطان الذي هو ظلام؟ فإن تعاطوا إقامة الدليل على حدث الظلام بشيء،
A issue in refuting the Magians as well. And it is said to them: 'If the eternity of light (al-nur), which is the Creator (al-Bari), is allowed, what prevents you from the eternity of shaytan, which is darkness (zalam)? Then if they attempt to adduce evidence for the temporality of darkness with something...'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage challenges the Magian dualism of light and darkness. Baqillani argues that if light (identified with God) is eternal, then there is no reason to deny the eternity of darkness. If they try to prove darkness is temporally originated (i.e., created), they will face difficulties. The argument is left incomplete in this window, but the challenge is clear: they cannot consistently maintain the eternity of light while denying the eternity of darkness without a decisive proof that darkness is originated.

Key Terms
النور (al-nur: light)
الظلام (al-zalam: darkness)
قِدَم (qidam: eternity)
الباري (al-Bari: the Creator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That light and darkness are coeternal and independent principles.
Correction: Baqillani does not assert that both are eternal; rather, he argues that if the Magians claim light is eternal, they must explain why darkness is not also eternal, exposing an inconsistency in their dualism.

Checkpoint
What is Baqillani's challenge to the Magians about darkness?
A) That darkness is not real.
B) That if light is eternal, why should darkness not be eternal?
C) That darkness is more powerful than light.
D) That darkness was created by light.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani exposes an inconsistency in Magian dualism by using a parity argument: if light (God) is eternal, there is no obvious reason to deny the eternity of darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you apply a double standard—holding others to a rule you don't follow yourself? Baqillani's challenge forces you to face inconsistency. Will you examine one such area today and commit to change?
?
You believe in the Oneness of God (tawhid). But do you ever act as if something else—money, status, comfort—has equal or greater claim to your ultimate allegiance? If Baqillani asked you about that 'other eternal,' what would you say?
?
The Magians couldn't justify treating darkness differently from light. Can you justify the gaps between what you profess and how you live? This lesson demands consistency between belief and action. What is one small step you will take today to close that gap?

The Dilemma of the Liar and the Origin of Evil
Baqillani poses a dilemma about who creates a liar claiming to be from Satan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a dilemma about a liar claiming to be from Satan to refute the dualist division of good and evil.

Core Idea
A single liar exposes the impossibility of separate sources for good and evil.

This passage presents a sharp dialectical objection against those who believe in two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). The author constructs a dilemma around a man who claims 'I am from the creation of Satan.' Whichever answer the dualist gives, it forces a contradiction that undermines their core doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the direct style: 'inform us about one whom we have heard say...'
2
Understand the dualist context
Recall that dualists (like Manichaeans) believe light (good) and darkness (evil) are separate, eternal principles. This objection targets that separation.
The word 'نور' refers to the good principle.
3
Analyze the first horn: if light created him
If the dualists say light created the man, then light would have created a liar (since he falsely claims to be from Satan) and someone who misattributes his own creation. This forces light to be capable of creating evil, collapsing the distinction.
The text: 'فقد خَلَقَ النُّورِ مَن كَذِبَ'
4
Analyze the second horn: if Satan created him
If they say Satan created the man, then the man is truthful (since he correctly attributes himself to Satan). This means Satan created a truthful good act, implying Satan can create good. Why then deny that all good comes from Satan? This also collapses the separation.
The text: 'فقد صَدَقَ هذا الناطق'
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes 'وهذا ترك دينكم' – this is abandoning your religion. The dilemma forces the dualist to abandon the core tenet of two separate sources.
Consider how a single, well-constructed dilemma can dismantle a whole worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أخرى عليهم ويقال لهم : خبرونا عمّن سمعناه يقول : أنا من خلق الشيطان من الذي خلقه؟ فإن قالوا : النور خلقه، قيل لهم : فقد خَلَقَ النُّورِ مَن كَذِبَ، وأضاف خلق نفسِهِ إلى غير خالقِهِ ؛ وإن جاز ذلك جاز أن يخلق الظالم الجائر، وجاز أن يخلق سائر الشرور ، وإن قالوا : الشيطان هو الذي خلق هذا القائل، قيل لهم : فقد صَدَقَ هذا الناطق ؛ فإن جاز أن يخلق الشيطانُ خيراً صادقاً عليه، فما أنكرتم أن يخلق سائر الخير وجميع فاعليه حتى يكون منه الخير والشر؟ وهذا ترك دينكم .
Another objection against them: And it is said to them: inform us about one whom we have heard say: 'I am from the creation of Satan' – who created him? If they say: light created him, it is said to them: then light created one who lied, and attributed the creation of himself to other than his creator; and if that is permissible, then it is permissible that light creates the unjust tyrant, and it is permissible that it creates all other evils. And if they say: Satan created this speaker, it is said to them: then this speaker has told the truth; and if it is permissible that Satan creates good that is truthful about him, why do you deny that he creates all other good and all its doers, so that good and evil both come from him? And this is abandoning your religion.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical objection (musa'ala) against the dualists who believe in two principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). The author presents a scenario: there is a person who says 'I am from the creation of Satan' – implying he was created by Satan. The dualists are asked: who created this person? Two answers are considered: (1) If they say light created him, then light would have created a liar (since the person falsely claims to be from Satan) and also would have created one who attributes his own creation to someone else (Satan). If light can create such evil, then it could create all evils, contradicting the dualist claim that light only creates good. (2) If they say Satan created him, then the speaker is truthful (since Satan actually created him), meaning Satan created a truthful good act. If Satan can create good, then he could create all good, removing the separation of principles. Either answer leads to contradictions that undermine the dualist religion. The conclusion 'وهذا ترك دينكم' (this is abandoning your religion) indicates the argument forces the dualist to give up their core tenets.

Key Terms
شيطان
نور
خلق
صدق
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is merely a tricky hypothetical with no real force.
Correction: The objection systematically shows that both possible answers lead to logical contradictions in the dualist worldview, proving its incoherence.

Checkpoint
What is the first possible answer to the question 'Who created the man who says he is from Satan's creation?' that the author presents?
A) The man created himself.
B) Light created him.
C) Darkness created him.
D) God created him.

Key Takeaway
The dualist system cannot coherently explain the existence of a liar who attributes himself to Satan without either making light the source of evil or Satan the source of good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself blaming your mistakes on 'the devil' or on your environment, are you not implicitly adopting a kind of dualism? How does Baqillani's argument force you to take full responsibility for your actions as solely answerable to the One Creator?
?
The person in the example makes a false claim about his origin. Have you ever deceived yourself by attributing your shortcomings to an external source, rather than acknowledging them as your own? What would it mean to stop lying to yourself and accept that good and evil in your life come from the same divine test?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone creates all things, including your evil deeds, how does that change the way you seek repentance? Are you willing to confront the discomfort that your own choices are part of a divine decree, or do you secretly wish some evils were outside His control so you could blame them elsewhere?

The Apostate's Conversion Unravels Dualism
Baqillani challenges dualists on whether God can create evil, using apostasy as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani uses the case of a converted Magian to refute the dualist belief that good and evil come from separate creators.

Core Idea
One person's conversion forces the dualists to contradict themselves.

Imam Baqillani continues his refutation of the dualists with a concrete example: a Magian who converts to Judaism. This forces the dualists to either admit that God creates evil or deny the obvious reality of personal change. This lesson walks through the logical traps Baqillani sets and shows why the dualist position ultimately collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall dialectical flow.
Notice the repeated pattern: if they say X, then Y follows.
2
Identify the two initial options
Baqillani first asks whether God can create an evil liar. The dualists must answer yes or no. Each answer leads to a trap.
These two branches form the backbone of the objection.
3
Trace the reduction arguments
Trace what happens if they say yes (they abandon their position) and if they say no (the apostate case follows). For the apostate, consider three possible attributions: created by Satan, created by God, or a different person.
Each attribution is shown to be absurd or self-defeating.
4
Reflect on the final absurdity
The dualists' last resort is denying real change in the apostate. Baqillani refutes this by appealing to self-evident experience, making further debate pointless.
This shows the dualist position contradicts necessity and sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أخرى عليهم ويقال لهم : هل يجوز أن يخلق الله شريراً كذاباً يعصيه ويشتمه ويفتري عليه؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا قولهم وقيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون خالقاً لجميع الشرور؟ وإن قالوا : لا ، قيل لهم : فخبرونا عن رجل كان مجوسياً دائناً بقولكم ثم تهوّدَ وانتقل عن المجوسية وأكفر أهلها، مِن خَلْقٍ مَن هو؟ فإن قالوا من خلقِ الشيطان، قيل : فقد فعل الشيطان خيراً، معتقداً للخير، برهة من الدهر ؛ وإن جاز ذلك جاز أن يخلق جميع الخير؛ وإن قالوا: من خلق الرحمن، قيل لهم : فقد خلق الرحمن الشرير الذي تهود وتزندق وكذب عليه ؛ وإن جاز ذلك جاز أن يفعل سائر الشرور. وإن قالوا : الذي تزندق وتهوّد غير الذي كان مجوسياً، قيل لهم : فعلى هذا ما انتقل أحدٌ قط عن حق اعتقده ؛ والمعتقد للمجوسية على ما هو عليه، وإن صار إلى التدين باليهودية والزندقة، لم يفارق ما اعتقده قط ولا برىء منه وهذا جحد الضرورة والحس لأن الإنسان يجد من نفسه اعتقاداً لغير ما كان معتقداً له وذَمَّه لما كان عليه بعد مدحه له ؛ فلا معنى لمناظرة من انتهى إلى هذا الحد
Another objection against them: And it is said to them: Is it permissible that God creates an evil liar who disobeys Him, reviles Him, and slanders Him? If they say: yes, they abandon their statement, and it is said to them: why then do you deny that He is the creator of all evils? And if they say: no, it is said to them: inform us about a man who was a Magian, adhering to your doctrine, then became a Jew, and transferred from Magianism and declared its people disbelievers – from whose creation is he? If they say: from the creation of Satan, it is said: then Satan has done a good deed, believing in good for a period of time; and if that is permissible, then it is permissible that he creates all good. And if they say: from the creation of the Merciful (i.e., God), it is said to them: then the Merciful has created the evil one who became a Jew and a heretic and lied about Him; and if that is permissible, then He can do all evils. And if they say: the one who became a heretic and a Jew is other than the one who was a Magian, it is said: on this basis, no one has ever transferred from a truth he believed; the one who believed in Magianism remains as he was, and even if he comes to adopt Judaism and heresy, he has never left what he believed nor been free from it – and this is a denial of necessity and sense, because a person finds in himself a belief in something other than what he used to believe and condemns what he was upon after having praised it. So there is no point in arguing with one who has reached this limit.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a second dialectical objection against the dualists. The author first asks whether God can create an evil liar. If the dualists say yes, they abandon their own position that God only creates good. If they say no, the author presents a concrete case: a man who was a Magian (believer in two principles) converts to Judaism and renounces his former religion. The dualists are asked: who created this apostate? Two answers are explored: (1) If they say Satan created him, then Satan has performed a good act by creating this man's belief in Judaism? Actually the argument is more subtle: the apostate 'believed in good' (i'tiqad al-khayr) for a time? Wait, the text says 'فقد فعل الشيطان خيراً، معتقداً للخير' meaning Satan did good, believing in good (i.e., the apostate's act of converting to Judaism might be considered evil by dualists, but the objection turns on the attribution). Actually the text says 'فقد فعل الشيطان خيراً' (Satan did good) – perhaps because the apostate's new belief is considered good? The logic: if Satan created the apostate's conversion, then Satan created a good act (since the conversion is from falsehood to truth? Or simply because the apostate now believes in a different religion). The point is that if Satan can create some good, he can create all good, collapsing dualism. (2) If they say God created him, then God created an evil apostate, so God can create all evil. The dualists might try to escape by saying the apostate is a different person from the original Magian – i.e., no real change. The author refutes this by appealing to self-evident experience: people do change their beliefs, they find themselves believing differently and condemning past beliefs. This reduction ad absurdum shows that the dualist position leads to denying obvious reality, making further debate pointless.

Key Terms
خلق الشرير (creation of the evil one)
تهويد (Judaism)
المجوس (Magians/Zoroastrians)
الانتقال (transfer/conversion)
الضرورة والحس (necessity and sense)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about free will vs determinism.
Correction: The objection is specifically aimed at the dualist principle that good and evil come from separate sources. The argument shows the logical impossibility of maintaining such a distinction in face of observable change.

Checkpoint
What is the third possible answer the dualists might give when asked who created the apostate?
A) The apostate created himself.
B) The apostate who became a Jew is a different person from the one who was a Magian.
C) God created him, but he was not originally evil.
D) The apostate's conversion is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that change of religion is a self-evident reality that dualists cannot explain without abandoning their core principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani argues that change of belief is self-evident. When was the last time you experienced a genuine shift in your convictions about God or religion? Did you attribute that change to your own effort or to God's guidance? How does this affect your understanding of divine control over all actions, including your own?
?
The dualist denies that a person can truly change, claiming the convert is a different person. In your own life, have you ever treated someone who changed their religion or beliefs as if they were somehow 'not the same person'? What does this lesson reveal about the importance of acknowledging sincere transformation?
?
If God creates both the believer and the apostate, what does that mean for how you view those who have left your faith? Do you see them as a product of God's creation or as purely their own choice? How does this shape your compassion and your prayers for them?

The Origin of Christian Sects and Paul's Deception
Baqillani narrates the origin of Christian sects and the role of Paul in dividing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's narrative of how Christian sects originated through the deception of Paul, and the key doctrinal corruptions introduced.

Core Idea
Christian divisions trace back to a single scheme of deception by Paul.

In this passage, Baqillani provides a historical account of the fragmentation of Christianity into numerous sects. He traces the origin to a plot by a Jew named Paul who pretended to convert to Christianity in order to corrupt it from within. This narrative serves as a polemical explanation for the divergence of Christian doctrine from the original teachings of Jesus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the full story. Notice the listing of sects and the dramatic narrative of Paul.
The passage is detailed; take your time.
2
Identify the three main sects
The passage mentions three major groups: Melkites, Nestorians, and Jacobites. Find them in the text and note how they are described.
3
Analyze Paul's role and doctrinal innovations
Paul is the central figure who introduces corrupt teachings: Trinity, divinity and humanity of Jesus, and others. Identify these innovations and how they led to further splits.
Reflect on the polemical nature of this account.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افترقت النصارى اثنتين وسبعين فرقة وكبار فرقهم ثلاثة : الملكانية، والنسطورية، واليعقوبية، وانشعبت منها الإليانية والبليارسية والمقد انوسية والسبالية والبوطينوسيه، والبولية، إلى سائر الفرق. وكان سبب تفرقهم ما ذكره المفسرون وأصحاب التواريخ وذلك أنهم قالوا: إن النصارى كانوا متمسكين بدين عيسى عليه السلام بعد ما رفع إلى السماء إحدى وثلاثين سنة، وكانوا يجبرون على الاستقامة إلى أن وقع بينهم وبين اليهود حرب . وكان في اليهود رجل اسمه بولس قتل منهم مقتلة عظيمة، ثم قال لليهود إن كان قوم عيسى على الحق ونحن قد كفرنا بهم يكون علينا غبن عظيم فإنهم يدخلون الجنة ونحن ندخل النار، ولكني احتال حيلة حتى أفسد عليهم دينهم، وكان له فرس اسمه عقاب وكان يقاتل عليه . فقام وعقر ذلك الفرس وأظهر الندم على ما كان منه ونثر التراب على رأسه ثم جاء إلى النصارى متندماً بظاهره فقالوا له من أنت؟ فقال: أنا بولس كنت أشد عدو لكم ولكني سمعت من السماء نداء أن توبتك لا تقبل إلا أن تتنصر. الآن تبت ورجعت إلى دينكم فأكرموه وأدخلوه كنيستهم فلازم بيتاً من بيوتها لم يخرج منه ليلا ولا نهاراً حتى تعلم الإنجيل، ثم خرج وقال : سمعت من السماء أن توبتك قد قبلت وأن صدقك قد عرف، وأنك قد أحببت وقبلت . ثم خرج إلى بيت المقدس واستخلف رجلا من نسطور وعلمه أن عیسی ومريم والإله كانوا ثلاثة ثم خرج إلى الروم وعلمهم اللاهوت والناسوت وقال لهم : إن عيسى لم يكن ناساً ثم صار ناساً، ولم يكن جسماً ثم صار جسماً، وكان ابن، وعلم يعقوب هذا القول ثم دعا رجلاً كان اسمه ملكا وقال له : أنت صاحبي خالصاً فإني أريد أن أفضي إليك سر ينبغي أن لا تترك نحلتك هذه وتدعو الخلق إليها فقد رأيت عيسى عليه السلام البارحة في المنام وكان راضياً عني. فينبغي أن لا ترجع عن نحلتك بحال. فإني أريد أن أتقرب إلى الله تعالى بقربان لرضاه عني أذبح نفسي قرباناً . ثم قام ودخل المذبح وزج نفسه . فلما كان اليوم الثالث من وفاته قام كل واحد من أولئك الثلاثة ودعا الناس إلى نحلته . وتبع كل واحد منهم جماعة من الناس وكانوا يتقاتلون فيما بينهم وبقي بينهم ذلك الخلاف . ولم يزالوا يختلفون حتى بلغ عدد فرقهم مثل ما نطق به الخبر المروي في هذا الباب. وكان مذهبهم مذهب أصحاب الهيولى. وكانوا في بعض دينهم مع اليهود، وفي بعضـه مـع النصارى، وابتدعوا من عند أنفسهم أموراً كثيرة تخالف الفريقين.
The Christians split into seventy-two sects, their major groups being three: the Melkites, the Nestorians, and the Jacobites. From them branched the Elyanaeans, the Bilyarsians, the Macedonians, the Sabellians, the Potinoseans, the Paulians, and the rest of the sects. The cause of their division was what the exegetes and historians mentioned: they said that the Christians were adhering to the religion of Jesus, peace be upon him, after he was raised to heaven for thirty-one years, and they were compelled to uprightness until a war occurred between them and the Jews. There was a man among the Jews named Paul who had killed a great number of them. Then he said to the Jews: If the people of Jesus are upon the truth and we have disbelieved in them, we will suffer a great loss, for they will enter Paradise and we will enter the Fire. But I will devise a scheme to corrupt their religion. He had a horse named 'Iqab on which he used to fight. So he stood and hamstrung that horse, and showed remorse for what he had done, and threw dust on his head. Then he came to the Christians with apparent remorse. They said: Who are you? He said: I am Paul, I was your worst enemy, but I heard a call from heaven that your repentance is not accepted unless you become a Christian. Now I have repented and returned to your religion. So they honored him and let him into their church. He stayed in one of its houses, not leaving night or day, until he learned the Gospel. Then he came out and said: I heard from heaven that your repentance has been accepted and your sincerity is known, and that you are loved and accepted. Then he went out to Jerusalem and appointed a man from Nestorius and taught him that Jesus, Mary, and God were three. Then he went out to Rome and taught them the divinity and humanity, and said to them: Jesus was not a human then became a human, and was not a body then became a body, and was a son. And he taught Jacob this statement. Then he called a man named Malika and said to him: You are my sincere companion, I want to confide in you a secret; you should not abandon your sect and should call people to it, for I saw Jesus peace be upon him last night in a dream and he was pleased with me. So you should not turn away from your sect at all. I want to draw near to Allah Almighty by a sacrifice to please Him, I will slaughter myself as a sacrifice. Then he stood and entered the sanctuary and stabbed himself. Then on the third day after his death, each of those three stood and called people to his sect. A group of people followed each of them, and they fought among themselves, and that disagreement remained. They continued to differ until the number of their sects reached what the reported tradition stated in this chapter. Their belief was the belief of the adherents of the matter [hyle]. They were with the Jews in some of their religion, and with the Christians in some of it, and they innovated many things from themselves that opposed both groups.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative about the origin of Christian sects. Baqillani reports that Christians split into seventy-two sects, with three major ones: Melkites, Nestorians, and Jacobites, and many others. The cause was the deception of a Jew named Paul. Paul, after killing many Christians, pretended to repent and infiltrated the Christian community. He learned the Gospel, then claimed divine acceptance. He then taught heretical doctrines: that Jesus, Mary, and God are three (Trinity), and that Jesus had divinity and humanity (lahut and nasut). He influenced Nestorius and a man named Jacob, and later another man named Malika, whom he instructed to maintain his sect. Paul then committed suicide as a supposed sacrifice. After his death, his followers each propagated their own sect, leading to conflict and multiplication of divisions. Their beliefs mixed elements from Jews and Christians, and they innovated many things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani's account of Paul is historically accurate.
Correction: This narrative is a polemical explanation, not necessarily historical fact. It is used to illustrate how Christian doctrine became corrupted.

Baqillani uses the story of Paul to explain the corruption of Christian doctrine and the origin of many sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Paul appeared outwardly remorseful and zealous, yet his inner intention was corrupt. In your own practice of faith, how do you ensure that your outward acts of worship are matched by sincere intention? Are there any religious habits you perform that you have not examined for sincerity?
?
The Christians initially had a unified religion but were gradually divided by false teachings. Think about your own knowledge of Islam: are there any beliefs or practices you hold that you have not verified against the Quran and authenticated Sunnah? What steps will you take to safeguard yourself from blind following of popular but unverified teachings?
?
Paul used his knowledge of the Gospel to corrupt it from within. This warns us about the misuse of religious knowledge. Have you ever used your knowledge of Islam to argue or manipulate? How can you ensure that your learning brings you closer to humility and service rather than arrogance and division?

The Origin of the Trinitarian Sect and Its Spread
Baqillani recounts a narrative about the origin of a Christian sect and its division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the narrative account of the origin of a Christian Trinitarian sect as described by Imam Baqillani, including its key doctrines and the events leading to its division.

Core Idea
A narrative reveals how a Trinitarian sect emerged through teachings about Jesus and internal conflict.

Imam Baqillani presents a historical narrative about the beginnings of a Christian Trinitarian sect. This passage describes how a teacher went to Rome, taught the doctrines of divinity and humanity regarding Jesus, and how after a self-sacrifice, the followers split into warring factions. The story illustrates the sect's origins and its doctrinal deviations.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the sequence of events and the key doctrines mentioned.
Notice that the story begins with a statement about Mary and God being three, which sets the stage for Trinitarian beliefs.
2
Identify the key claims about Jesus
The passage states that Jesus was not a human then became human, and was not a body then became a body. Also, he was called a son. Reflect on these claims.
These claims are foundational to the sect's doctrine.
3
Observe the division and aftermath
Note how after Jacob's self-sacrifice, three individuals each called people to their own sect, leading to conflict and many divisions. Also note the reference to the 'followers of hyle' and the borrowing from Jews and Christians.
This shows how the sect fragmented and adopted various beliefs.

ومريم والإله كانوا ثلاثة ثم خرج إلى الروم وعلمهم اللاهوت والناسوت وقال لهم: إن عيسى لم يكن ناساً ثم صار ناساً، ولم يكن جسماً ثم صار جسماً، وكان ابن، وعلم يعقوب هذا القول ثم دعا رجلاً كان اسمه ملكا وقال له: أنت صاحبي خالصاً فإني أريد أن أفضي إليك سر ينبغي أن لا تترك نحلتك هذه وتدعو الخلق إليها فقد رأيت عيسى عليه السلام البارحة في المنام وكان راضياً عني. فينبغي أن لا ترجع عن نحلتك بحال. فإني أريد أن أتقرب إلى الله تعالى بقربان لرضاه عني أذبح نفسي قرباناً. ثم قام ودخل المذبح وزج نفسه. فلما كان اليوم الثالث من وفاته قام كل واحد من أولئك الثلاثة ودعا الناس إلى نحلته. وتبع كل واحد منهم جماعة من الناس وكانوا يتقاتلون فيما بينهم وبقي بينهم ذلك الخلاف. ولم يزالوا يختلفون حتى بلغ عدد فرقهم مثل ما نطق به الخبر المروي في هذا الباب. وكان مذهبهم مذهب أصحاب الهيولى. وكانوا في بعض دينهم مع اليهود، وفي بعضـه مـع النصارى، وابتدعوا من عند أنفسهم أموراً كثيرة تخالف الفريقين.
And Mary and God were three, then he went out to Rome and taught them divinity and humanity, and said to them: Indeed, Jesus was not a human then he became a human, and was not a body then he became a body, and he was a son. And Jacob taught this statement, then he called a man whose name was Malka and said to him: You are my pure companion, for I want to confide to you a secret: you should not abandon this sect of yours and call the creation to it, for I saw Jesus, peace be upon him, last night in a dream, and he was pleased with me. So you should not revert from your sect at all. For I want to draw near to God Almighty by a sacrifice for His pleasure: I will sacrifice myself. Then he stood and entered the altar and threw himself. When the third day after his death came, each of those three stood and called the people to his sect. Each one of them was followed by a group of people, and they fought among themselves, and that disagreement remained among them. They continued to differ until the number of their sects reached what the reported tradition in this chapter spoke of. Their doctrine was the doctrine of the followers of hyle. And in some of their religion they were with the Jews, and in some of it with the Christians, and they innovated from themselves many things contradicting the two groups.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage narrates a story about the origin of a Christian Trinitarian sect. It begins by stating that Mary and God were three (i.e., a trinity), then someone (likely a teacher) went to Rome and taught them the doctrine of 'divinity and humanity' (lahut and nasut), claiming that Jesus was not a human then became human, was not a body then became a body, and was a son. Then a person named Jacob taught this statement and called a man named Malka, telling him that he saw Jesus in a dream and Jesus was pleased with him, so Malka should not abandon his sect. Jacob then sacrificed himself. After three days, three individuals each called people to their own sect, leading to conflict and division until many sects formed. Their doctrine is described as that of the 'followers of hyle' (ashab al-hayula), i.e., materialists, and they borrowed from both Jews and Christians while innovating many things contrary to both.

Why Not Four Hypostases? Baqillani's Dialectical Critique
Baqillani critiques the Trinity by questioning why three hypostases and not four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical argument against the Christian Trinity, where he shows that accepting the opponents' criteria for three hypostases logically leads to either four or two.

Core Idea
If three, why not four? If power equals life, why not knowledge? Inconsistency exposed.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani engages the Christian doctrine of the Trinity using a dialectical method. Instead of outright rejection, he adopts the opponents' framework and presses the logic: if attributes necessitate hypostases, why stop at three? This lesson walks through the exchange, revealing the internal inconsistency of the Trinity claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the back-and-forth structure: question, answer, counter-question.
2
Identify the initial claim and justification
The opponent claims God is three hypostases: substance, life, and knowledge. Find how they justify this based on God being living and knowing.
The opponent's logic: God is a substance, living, knowing → three hypostases.
3
Trace the author's first counter-argument
Baqillani asks: why not four? Because God is also powerful, requiring a hypostasis of power. See how he extends the opponent's logic.
4
Follow the opponent's attempt to evade
The opponent claims power is identical to life, so only three hypostases. Then Baqillani turns the tables: if power equals life, why not knowledge equals life, reducing to two?
5
Observe the final contradiction
The opponent tries to distinguish knowledge from life based on changeability. Baqillani applies the same reasoning to power, showing power must also be distinct, thus four hypostases. The opponent's position is inconsistent.
The key move: the criterion used to separate knowledge from life also separates power from life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام عليهم في الأقانيم
يقال لهم : لم زعمتم أن الباري سبحانه ثلاثة أقانيم دون أن تقولوا إنه أربعة وعشرة وأكثر من ذلك؟ فإن قالوا من قبل أنه قد ثبت أن الباري سبحانه موجود جوهر وثبت أنه حي وأنه عالم فوجب أنه واحد ثلاثة أقانيم منها الجوهر الموجود ومنها العلم والحياة جوهر لأن الحي العالم لا يكون حيا عالما حتى يكون ذا حياة وعلم ; فوجب أن الأقانيم ثلاثة ; فيقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من أن تكون الأقانيم أربعة؟ لأننا نقول إن القديم موجود حي عالم قادر ; والقادر لا بد له من قدرة ; فوجب أن تكون الأقانيم أربعة .
فإن قالوا : القدرة هي الحياة، فهما أقنوم واحد ; قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون العلم هو الحياة؟ فوجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه أقنومين
فإن قالوا : قد ينقص العلم ويزيد ويوجد ويعدم، والحياة باقية بحالها، فوجب أن يكون العلم ليس من معنى الحياة في شيء; قيل لهم : فكذلك قد تنقص القدرة وتزيد وتعدم جملة، ثم توجد والحياة بحالها ; فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة وبخلاف معناه .
The discussion against them concerning the hypostases. It is said to them: Why do you claim that the Creator, glorified be He, is three hypostases, rather than saying He is four, ten, or more than that? If they say: Because it is established that the Creator, glorified be He, exists as a substance, and it is established that He is living and that He is knowing, so it necessitates that He is one, three hypostases: the existing substance, and knowledge and life, because the living, knowing being cannot be living and knowing unless he has life and knowledge. So it necessitates that the hypostases are three. It is said to them: Why do you deny that the hypostases are four? Because we say that the Eternal is existing, living, knowing, powerful; and the powerful must have power; so it necessitates that the hypostases are four. If they say: Power is life, so they are one hypostasis. It is said to them: So why do you deny that knowledge is life? Then the Creator, glorified be He, would be two hypostases. If they say: Knowledge may decrease and increase, and exists and ceases, while life remains in its state, so it necessitates that knowledge is not in any way of the meaning of life. It is said to them: Similarly, power may decrease and increase and cease entirely, then exist, while life remains in its state; so it necessitates that power is other than life and contrary to its meaning.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian doctrine of the Trinity (أقانيم). The author asks why the opponent claims that God is three hypostases (aqanim) rather than four or more. The opponent replies that it is established that God exists as a substance (جوهر), is living, and is knowing, so He must have three hypostases: substance, life, and knowledge, because a living knowing being must have life and knowledge. The author then asks why not four, adding power, because God is also powerful and power requires a hypostasis. The opponent responds that power is the same as life, so they are one hypostasis. The author retorts: why not say knowledge is the same as life, reducing to two hypostases? The opponent distinguishes by saying that knowledge can increase, decrease, and be absent, while life remains constant, showing knowledge is different from life. The author then turns the argument: similarly, power can increase, decrease, and be absent, so it must be different from life, necessitating four hypostases. The opponent's position is thus shown to be inconsistent.

Key Terms
أقنوم
جوهر
حياة
علم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a general rejection of the Trinity without engaging its logic.
Correction: Baqillani uses the opponents' own framework and criteria to show internal inconsistency, not a simple denial.

Checkpoint
What does the author suggest would be the consequence if the opponent argues that knowledge is distinct from life because knowledge can be gained and lost?
A) That power must also be distinct from life
B) That God would have only two hypostases
C) That life and knowledge are the same
D) That the Trinity is proven

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the Christian Trinity is rationally incoherent because the same reasoning that yields three hypostases can be extended to yield four or reduced to two, exposing the opponent's inconsistent application of criteria.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief, do you ever find yourself using criteria that you would reject if applied to other parts of your belief system? Where in your life might you be doing this right now, and will you honestly examine it today?
?
Baqillani's method reveals that sometimes we accept claims because they are familiar, not because they are consistent. Is there a belief you hold about God or religion that you have never subjected to the same logical scrutiny you apply to others' beliefs? What would it cost you to test it?
?
The opponent here tried to protect three hypostases by adjusting arguments mid-stream. When your worldview is challenged, do you instinctively patch it with new justifications, or do you allow the challenge to reshape you? Will you pray for the courage to follow truth even if it dismantles your comfort?

Why Not Four Hypostases?
Baqillani questions the number three and argues for four hypostases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical critique of the Trinity by adding the attribute of power undermines the Christian justification for three hypostases.

Core Idea
If life and knowledge require hypostases, why not power?

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani applies a simple but devastating logical test to the Christian doctrine of the Trinity. He notes that if the attributes of life and knowledge each require a separate hypostasis, then consistency demands that power should also have its own. This exposes the arbitrariness of limiting the hypostases to three.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of argument.
2
Identify the Christian position
The opponent argues that God is existent substance, living, and knowing, so three hypostases follow.
Note the terms: جوهر, حي, عالم.
3
Spot the counter-argument
Baqillani adds the attribute of power (قادر) and says power requires a hypostasis, making four.
This exposes inconsistency in the criterion.
4
Consider the implication
If the opponent's method is applied consistently, any attribute could multiply hypostases indefinitely.
This challenges the entire basis for the Trinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام عليهم في الأقانيم
يقال لهم : لم زعمتم أن الباري سبحانه ثلاثة أقانيم دون أن تقولوا إنه أربعة وعشرة وأكثر من ذلك؟ فإن قالوا من قبل أنه قد ثبت أن الباري سبحانه موجود جوهر وثبت أنه حي وأنه عالم فوجب أنه واحد ثلاثة أقانيم منها الجوهر الموجود ومنها العلم والحياة جوهر لأن الحيَّ العالم لا يكون حيّاً عالماً حتى يكون ذا حياة وعلم ; فوجب أن الأقانيم ثلاثة ; فيقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من أن تكون الأقانيم أربعة? لأننا نقول إن القديم موجود حي عالم قادر ; والقادر لا بدّ له من قدرة ; فوجب أن تكون الأقانيم أربعة .
The discourse against them concerning the hypostases. It is said to them: Why do you claim that the Creator, exalted is He, is three hypostases, and you do not say that He is four, ten, and more than that? If they say: Because it has been established that the Creator, exalted is He, is an existent substance, and it has been established that He is living and that He is knowing, so it is necessary that He is one [in] three hypostases: from them the existent substance, and from them knowledge and life—a substance—because the living, knowing [being] is not living, knowing until he possesses life and knowledge; so it is necessary that the hypostases are three. Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [saying] that the hypostases are four? Because we say that the Eternal is existent, living, knowing, powerful; and the powerful must have power; so it is necessary that the hypostases are four.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a refutation of the Christian doctrine of the hypostases (الأقانيم). The author first states the Christian claim that God is three hypostases and immediately challenges why they stop at three, asking why not four, ten, or more. The opponent responds by grounding the three in the attributes of existence, life, and knowledge, arguing that these three attributes require distinct hypostases. The author then counters by adding power (قدرة) to the list of divine attributes, concluding that if the same reasoning is applied, power would demand a fourth hypostasis. This sets up the core logical problem: the criterion for what makes an attribute a hypostasis is arbitrary.

Key Terms
الأقانيم
الباري
جوهر
حي
عالم
قادر
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to the Christian Trinity.
Correction: It is a logical critique of any attempt to multiply persons based on attributes, not just the Trinity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the hypostases should be four?
A) Because God is also powerful and power requires a separate hypostasis.
B) Because the Christians themselves admit to four attributes.
C) Because the number four is more perfect than three.
D) Because the opponent's reasoning is based on ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The Christian criterion for identifying hypostases is arbitrary, as demonstrated by adding the attribute of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold beliefs about Allah, do you ever stop to ask why you believe them? Are your reasons consistent, or do you accept some claims just because they are familiar?
?
If you were challenged to list all the necessary attributes of Allah, would you stop at a certain number? How would you defend that number against someone who adds more? Is your defense based on revelation or rational reasoning?
?
The Christian argument for three hypostases fails because of arbitrariness. Are there any areas in your own faith where you hold a belief without a solid reason? Will you examine them today?

Reducing Hypostases by Reductio
Baqillani reduces the hypostases to two by questioning the distinction of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum to expose inconsistency in the opponent's identification of divine attributes.

Core Idea
One reduction leads to another: if power equals life, why not knowledge too?

In this brief dialectical exchange, Baqillani anticipates an objection from his opponents who attempt to reduce the number of divine hypostases by identifying power with life. Rather than rejecting their move outright, he cleverly turns their own logic against them, showing that if one attribute can be reduced to life, then so can another—leading to an even smaller number of hypostases than they intended. This lesson unpacks the rhetorical and logical strategy behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the structure: opponent's claim followed by the author's counter.
The Arabic uses 'فإن قالوا... قيل لهم' a typical dialectical formula.
2
Identify the logical move
The opponent claims power is life, making one hypostasis. The author responds: why not also say knowledge is life? This applies the same logic to another attribute, leading to a different conclusion (two hypostases). Recognize that this is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's principle (identifying attributes) leads to an unwanted outcome.
The author does not accept the identification; he uses it against them.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is this argument effective? It shows that if the opponent's method of identification is allowed, it can be extended arbitrarily, contradicting their own position. This demonstrates the inconsistency in selectively identifying only some attributes.
This illustrates a key kalam method: showing that an opponent's premise leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : القدرة هي الحياة، فهما أقنوم واحد ; قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون العلم هو الحياة? فوجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه أقنومين
If they say: 'Power is life, so they are one hypostasis,' it is said to them: 'Then what prevents you from [saying] that knowledge is life? So it is necessary that the Creator, exalted is He, [be] two hypostases.'
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a quick dialectical exchange. The opponent, faced with the argument for four hypostases, tries to reduce the count by identifying power with life, claiming they are one hypostasis. The author immediately turns this against them: if power can be reduced to life, why not also reduce knowledge to life? If both are the same, then only two hypostases remain (substance and life). This shows the inconsistency in the opponent's attempt to selectively identify attributes.

Key Terms
القدرة
الحياة
العلم
أقنوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage shows that Baqillani believes there are only two hypostases.
Correction: The passage is a reductio ad absurdum; Baqillani does not endorse the two-hypostasis view. He is showing that the opponent's logic leads to an absurdity, not advocating it himself.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that power is the same as life?
A) He agrees and says there are two hypostases total.
B) He asks why knowledge cannot also be identified with life, leading to two hypostases.
C) He argues that power is distinct from life based on scripture.
D) He changes the subject to other attributes.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that arbitrarily identifying attributes leads to unacceptable consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were the opponent in this debate and Baqillani turned your own argument against you, how would that feel? What does this teach you about the danger of making arbitrary assumptions in theology?
?
This lesson shows a logical reductio that forces consistency. Reflect on your own beliefs: is there any position you hold that, if applied consistently, would lead to a conclusion you reject? What would it take for you to re-examine that position?
?
Baqillani's argument reveals that accepting one identification of attributes leads to a chain reaction. In your own study of Islamic theology, are you careful to check that your understanding of divine attributes is internally consistent, or do you sometimes accept pieces of different systems without testing their coherence?

Arguments from Variability and Absence of Power
Baqillani uses the variability and absence of power to distinguish it from life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the variability and occasional absence of power (while life persists) to demonstrate that power is a distinct attribute from life.

Core Idea
What can come and go cannot be the same as what always stays.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to Christian theologians who argued that because knowledge can increase, decrease, and even vanish (as in sleep), it cannot be identical to life. He cleverly turns their own reasoning against them: power also fluctuates and can be entirely absent during paralysis, while life remains. This proves that power is also distinct from life—a key step in establishing the four divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from variation
Read the first part where the opponent's claim about knowledge is presented, and then Baqillani's parallel application to power.
Notice the structure: 'If they say... it is said to them...'
2
Read the argument from absence
Read the second part where the opponent uses sleep/fainting, and Baqillani responds with paralysis.
The pattern is identical: the opponent's own method is used against them.
3
Identify the conclusion
Find the phrase 'فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة وأن الأقانيم أربعة'. This is the conclusion: power is other than life, and the hypostases are four.
The hypostases refer to the Christian Trinity plus one? Actually, in context, Baqillani is arguing that if knowledge and power are separate, then there are four distinct attributes.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why Baqillani uses the opponent's own argument. What does this achieve?
It strengthens the case by using accepted premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : قد ينقص العلم ويزيد ويوجد ويعدم، والحياة باقية بحالها، فوجب أن يكون العلم ليس من معنى الحياة في شيء; قيل لهم : فكذلك قد تنقص القدرة وتزيد وتعدم جملة، ثم توجد والحياة بحالها ; فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة وبخلاف معناه .
فإن قالوا قد يبطل العلم جملة في حالة النوم والغشي والإنسان حي ; قيل لهم : وكذلك قد تبطل القدرة جملة حتى لا يقدر الإنسان على تحريك يده أو لسانه أو إيماء بعض جوارحه، وهو حي في تلك الحال فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة وأن الأقانيم أربعة . فإن قالوا : دخول حرف المبالغة في صفة العالم في قولنا عالم وأعلم منه واستحالة المبالغة في صفة الحيّ والتفضيل بين الحيين دليل على أن العلم ليس من الحياة في شيء، قيل لهم : فقولوا لأجل هذا بعينه : إن القدرة غير الحياة لأننا قد نبالغ في صفة القادر ونقول: قادر وأقدر منه، ولا نقول : حي وأحيا منه ; فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة.
If they say: 'Knowledge may decrease and increase, exist and cease to exist, while life remains in its state, so it is necessary that knowledge is not at all from the meaning of life,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, power may decrease and increase and cease to exist entirely, then exist while life remains in its state; so it is necessary that power is other than life and contrary to its meaning.' If they say: 'Knowledge may entirely cease in the state of sleep and fainting while the human is alive,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, power may entirely cease so that the human cannot move his hand or tongue or gesture with some of his limbs, while he is alive in that state, so it is necessary that power is other than life and that the hypostases are four.' If they say: 'The entry of the letter of exaggeration in the attribute of the knowing in our saying 'knowing' and 'more knowing than him' and the impossibility of exaggeration in the attribute of the living and the comparison between the living indicate that knowledge is not at all from life,' it is said to them: 'Then say for this very reason that power is other than life, because we may exaggerate in the attribute of the powerful and say 'powerful' and 'more powerful than him,' and we do not say 'living' and 'more living than him'; so it is necessary that power is other than life.'
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct arguments to prove that power (قدرة) is a distinct attribute from life (حياة), and by extension that the hypostases should be four. First argument: knowledge can increase and decrease, and can be absent while life persists; the opponent uses this to show knowledge is not life. The author turns the argument around: power also increases and decreases and can cease entirely while life remains, so power is not life either. Second argument: knowledge can be absent during sleep or fainting while the person is still alive; similarly, power can be absent (e.g., paralysis) while the person is alive. Third argument: the comparative and superlative forms (المبالغة) are used for 'knowing' (عالم وأعلم) and for 'powerful' (قادر وأقدر) but not for 'living' (no one says أحيا), indicating that knowledge and power share a feature that life lacks, so they are distinct. Each argument concludes that power is other than life, and thus the hypostases are four.

Key Terms
ينقص
يزيد
يوجد
يعدم
النوم
الغشي
القدرة
الحياة
الأقانيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if power can be absent while life is present, then power is a temporary attribute that God does not have.
Correction: Baqillani is not denying that God has power; he is arguing that power is a distinct attribute from life, but both are eternal and necessary in God.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani conclude from the second argument about power?
A) Power is not necessary for life.
B) Power is distinct from life and there are four hypostases.
C) Power is identical to knowledge.
D) Power ceases entirely when life ceases.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that power is a distinct attribute from life by showing that power can vary and be absent while life remains constant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a loss of physical ability—like illness or weakness—do you feel your connection to God's power weaken? How does Baqillani's argument challenge you to separate your own fluctuating abilities from the unchanging divine attribute of power?
?
If knowledge and power can be absent in you while you are alive, what does that tell you about your dependency on God for these attributes? In what area of your life are you unconsciously relying on your own power rather than acknowledging it as a separate gift from the Life-Giver?
?
Baqillani uses the example of sleep and paralysis to show that life persists without knowledge or power. Have you ever experienced a moment when you were alive but felt completely powerless? How did that experience shape (or fail to shape) your understanding of God's life and power as distinct from yours?

The Superlative Forms Argument
Baqillani uses the absence of superlative for 'living' to prove power is distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the existence of superlative forms for 'knowing' and 'powerful' but not for 'living' to argue that power and knowledge are distinct from life.

Core Idea
If life were the source of power, we could say 'more living,' but we don't.

The opponents try to use a linguistic argument: because we can say 'more knowing' (أعلم) but not 'more living' (أحيا), knowledge must be something additional to life. Baqillani applies the same logic to power: we can say 'more powerful' (أقدر) but not 'more living,' so power too is distinct from life. This linguistic observation reinforces the previous arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's linguistic argument
The opponent claims that the ability to form a superlative for 'knowing' but not for 'living' shows knowledge is not life.
This is a subtle argument based on Arabic usage.
2
See Baqillani's response
Baqillani says: apply the same reasoning to power. We say 'more powerful' but not 'more living.' Thus power is not life either.
The structure is again: if they say... it is said to them...
3
Understand the linguistic point
The superlative form (المبالغة) indicates that the attribute can be intensified, which is not the case for life.
In theology, life is an essential attribute that does not admit of degrees.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is the same: power is other than life.
This third argument reinforces the earlier ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If they say: 'Knowledge may decrease and increase, exist and cease to exist, while life remains in its state, so it is necessary that knowledge is not at all from the meaning of life,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, power may decrease and increase and cease to exist entirely, then exist while life remains in its state; so it is necessary that power is other than life and contrary to its meaning.' First argument: knowledge can increase and decrease, and can be absent while life persists; the opponent uses this to show knowledge is not life. Extension to More Attributes
Baqillani extends the argument to will, remaining, hearing, seeing, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani extends the argument for hypostases to additional divine attributes and refutes Christian objections.

Core Idea
The logic of hypostases leads to absurd multiplication if applied consistently.

Imam Baqillani continues his debate with Christians by extending the same logical pattern to more divine attributes. He shows that if the opponent insists on deducing hypostases from attributes, there is no stopping at three—the same reasoning would yield many more. The passage then addresses several objections the opponent might raise, turning their own logic against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the new attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. List the five additional attributes Baqillani introduces beyond the initial three.
The attributes are will, remaining, hearing, seeing, and speech.
2
Trace the argument pattern
Notice how Baqillani repeats the same reasoning: each attribute necessitates a distinct hypostasis. This reveals the opponent's inconsistency.
3
Analyze the objections and responses
Examine each objection the opponent might raise and how Baqillani turns it back on them, showing that their own principles lead to more hypostases or to absurdity.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن تكون الأقانيم خمسة وعشرة? لأننا نقول : إن الباري موجود حي عالم قادر ; ونقول: إنه مريد وباق وسميع وبصير ومتكلم ; والباقي السميع البصير المتكلم المريد لا يكون كذلك إلا لوجود بقاء وإرادة وسمع وبصر وكلام . فإن قالوا البقاء هو هو، قيل لهم : والحياة والعلم هما هو? فقولوا إنه أقنوم واحد. فإن قالوا : الكلام والإرادة فعل من أفعال المتكلم المريد، قيل لهم : وكذلك العلم فعل من أفعال العالم ; فقولوا : إنه أقنومان. فإن قالوا : قد يعلم بالعلم من لم يفعله، قيل لهم : وقد يريد بالإرادة ويتكلم بالكلام من لا يفعله ; وكذلك إن قالوا سمع الباري سبحانه وبصره هو نفس علمه فوجب أنهما ليس
And likewise it is said to them: 'What prevents you from [saying] that the hypostases are five and ten? Because we say that the Creator is existent, living, knowing, powerful; and we say: He is willing, remaining, hearing, seeing, and speaking; and the remaining, the hearing, the seeing, the speaking, the willing are not so except by the existence of remaining, will, hearing, sight, and speech.' If they say: 'Remaining is identical [to something],' it is said to them: 'Are life and knowledge identical? Then say that He is one hypostasis.' If they say: 'Speech and will are an act of the speaker and willer,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, knowledge is an act of the knower; then say that there are two hypostases.' If they say: 'One may know by knowledge without [himself] performing it,' it is said to them: 'And one may will by will and speak by speech without performing it.' And likewise, if they say: 'The hearing and sight of the Creator, exalted is He, are the same as His knowledge,' so it is necessary that they are not...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
In this passage, the author extends the argument to additional divine attributes: will (إرادة), remaining/eternity (بقاء), hearing (سمع), seeing (بصر), and speech (كلام). He asks why the hypostases are not five or ten, since each of these attributes, following the same logic, would require a distinct hypostasis. The author then anticipates and refutes possible objections: (1) If the opponent says 'remaining is identical (to something else),' the author retorts: 'Then are life and knowledge identical? If so, you should say one hypostasis.' (2) If the opponent says 'speech and will are acts of the agent,' the author points out that knowledge could also be considered an act, leading to two hypostases. (3) If the opponent says 'one may know by knowledge without performing it,' the author notes the same applies to will and speech. The passage ends abruptly while addressing the claim that hearing and sight are the same as knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies that God actually has many separate hypostases.
Correction: Baqillani is not asserting multiple hypostases; he is showing that the opponent's method for determining hypostases, if applied consistently, leads to an absurd multiplication, thereby refuting their position.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What change will you make today to live more consciously in His presence?

The Argument from Nullification of Knowledge and Power in Sleep
Baqillani argues that nullification of power in sleep proves power is distinct from life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the nullification of power in sleep to argue that power is distinct from life, and how this refutes the Christian claim of three hypostases.

Core Idea
Power's absence in sleep proves it is distinct from life — making four hypostases.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to a trinitarian objection that knowledge can be absent in sleep while life remains. He turns the argument around: power is also absent in sleep, yet life remains, so power must be distinct from life. This leads to the conclusion that there are at least four hypostases (essence, life, knowledge, power), not three. Understanding this argument helps clarify the Islamic critique of Christian theological categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part: the claim that knowledge is nullified in sleep while life remains. Note that this is the opponent's starting point.
2
Find Baqillani's reply
Read the second part: he applies the same logic to power, which is also nullified in sleep. Understand that the parallel forces the opponent to accept that power is distinct from life.
3
Draw the conclusion
Connect the dots: if knowledge and power are both distinct from life, then the hypostases are at least four (essence, life, knowledge, power). This undercuts the Christian claim of three.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask: why does this work? Because the opponent's own reasoning (nullification indicates distinction) is applied consistently. This shows the power of logical consistency in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا قد يبطل العلم جملة في حالة النوم والغشي والإنسان حي؛ قيل لهم: وكذلك قد تبطل القدرة جملة حتى الإنسان لا يقدر على تحريك يده أو لسانه أو إيماء بعض جوارحه، وهو حي في تلك الحال فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة وأن الأقانيم أربعة.
If they say: knowledge is completely nullified in the state of sleep and fainting, while the human is alive; it is said to them: likewise power is completely nullified, such that the human is unable to move his hand or tongue or to gesture with some of his limbs, while he is alive in that state; so it is necessary that power is other than life, and that the hypostases are four.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument against the Christian trinitarian doctrine. The objection claims that knowledge is sometimes nullified (e.g., in sleep or fainting) even though a person remains alive, implying knowledge is distinct from life. The answer uses the same logic: power is also nullified in such states (the person cannot move), yet remains alive; therefore power is distinct from life. If life, knowledge, and power are each distinct, then there must be at least four hypostases (life, knowledge, power, and essence), contradicting the claim of three hypostases.

Key Terms

قدرة
حياة
أقنوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument disproves that God has knowledge or power, or that it denies the unity of divine attributes.
Correction: Baqillani is not denying divine attributes; he is using a human example to test the internal consistency of the Christian hypostasis model. The argument only shows that if the opponent's reasoning is applied consistently, it leads to four hypostases, not three.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge and power are the same as life.
B) Power is distinct from life, leading to at least four hypostases.
C) Sleep proves that humans are not alive.
D) The hypostases are five.

Key Takeaway
In sleep, power is nullified while life persists, proving power is distinct from life — a point that refutes the trinitarian claim of three hypostases.

Elative Forms and Divine Attribute Distinction
Baqillani uses comparative adjectives to prove power is distinct from life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Arabic grammatical elative forms to distinguish divine attributes like power from life.

Core Idea
Elative forms reveal essential attribute distinctions.

Baqillani continues his debate with Christian trinitarians by turning their own linguistic argument against them. The Christians argued that because we can say 'more knowledgeable' but not 'more living', knowledge is distinct from life. Baqillani responds that the same logic proves power is also distinct from life, further undermining their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Christian opponents present their argument using elative forms for knowledge and life.
Notice they claim the elative shows knowledge is not from life.
2
Identify the linguistic claim
Focus on the phrases 'عالم وأعلم منه' vs 'حي وأحيا منه' to see how Arabic grammar treats these attributes differently.
The elative form is possible for 'knowing' but not for 'living'.
3
Read Baqillani's answer
Read the second part where Baqillani turns the argument back: the same logic applies to power ('قادر وأقدر منه').
4
See the reasoning
Understand that the same linguistic pattern (elative possible for power, not for life) forces the conclusion that power is distinct from life.
Baqillani uses their own method against them.
5
Understand the theological implication
Reflect on how this argument undermines the Christian doctrine of the trinity by increasing the number of distinct attributes.
The logic would force an infinite regress of distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: دخول حرف المبالغة في صفة العالم في قولنا عالم وأعلم منه واستحالة المبالغة في صفة الحيّ والتفضيل بين الحيين دليل على أن العلم ليس من الحياة في شيء، قيل لهم: فقولوا لأجل هذا بعينه: إن القدرة غير الحياة لأننا قد نبالغ في صفة القادر ونقول: قادر وأقدر منه، ولا نقول: حي وأحيا منه؛ فوجب أن تكون القدرة غير الحياة.
If they say: the entry of the intensive particle in the description of the knower in our saying 'knowing' and 'more knowing than him' and the impossibility of intensiveness in the description of the living and preference between the living is evidence that knowledge is not from life at all, it is said to them: then say for precisely this same reason that power is other than life, because we may use intensiveness in the description of the powerful and say 'powerful' and 'more powerful than him', and we do not say 'living' and 'more living than him'; so it is necessary that power is other than life.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the trinity with another objection-and-answer. The objection notes that in Arabic, we can use the elative form ('af'al') for the attribute of knowledge (we say 'more knowledgeable') but not for life (we do not say 'more living'). This shows knowledge is not identical to life. The answer turns the argument back: the same reasoning applies to power (we say 'more powerful' but not 'more living'), so power must also be distinct from life. This again increases the number of hypostases.

Key Terms
