Christian Views on the Union: Indwelling, Mixture, and Temple
Baqillani lists early Christian views on the union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main Christian views on the union of the Word with Christ's body as reported by Baqillani: indwelling, mixture, and instrumental indwelling.

Core Idea
Christians disagree on whether the union is indwelling, mixture, or purely instrumental.

Baqillani opens his discussion by noting that Christian expressions about the union differ widely. He then lists several distinct positions, each with its own implications. This lesson presents the first set of views, from simple indwelling to physical mixture and the temple analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Christian views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Baqillani systematically lists different Christian groups and their positions.
The passage uses terms like حلول, اختلاط, and هيكل.
2
Identify the first two views
Identify the view that the Word indwelled the body, and the view that it is a physical mixture where the Word turned into flesh.
The Jacobites are mentioned as holding the mixture view.
3
Understand the mixture analogy
Notice that many compare the union to mixing water with wine or milk, implying a literal blending.
4
Examine the instrumental view
Some Christians said the Word used the body as a temple or instrument for management and manifestation, not mixture.
This view avoids the implications of physical change.
5
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why these different views are problematic from an Islamic perspective. Baqillani implies they are contradictory.
This sets up the need for a sound understanding of Tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام عليهم في معنى الاتحاد. وقد اختلفت عباراتهم عن معنى الاتحاد ؛ فقال منهم : معنى الاتحاد أن الكلمة التي الابن حلت جسد المسيح عليه السلام. وقالت طائفة أخرى وهم اليعاقبة وكثير منهم : إن الاتحاد هو اختلاط وامتزاج، وزعمت اليعقوبية أن كلمة الله انقلبت لحماً ودماً بالاتحاد. وزعم كثير منهم، أعني اليعقوبية والنسطورية أن اتحاد الكلمة بالناسوت اختلاط وامتزاج كاختلاط الماء وامتزاجه بالخمر واللبن إذا صُبّ فيهما ومزج بهما. وزعم قوم منهم أن معنى اتحاد الكلمة بالناسوت الذي هو الجسد هو اتحادها له هيكلاً ومحلاً وتدبيرها الأشياء عليه وظهورها فيه دون غيره. واختلفوا في معنى ظهور الكلمة في الهيكل وادّراعها له وإظهار التدبير عليه، فقال أكثرهم : معنى ذلك أنها حلّته ومازجته واختلطت به اختلاط الخمر واللبن بالماء عند امتزاجهما. وقال قوم منهم : إن ظهور الكلمة في الجسد واتحادها به ليس على معنى المزاج والاختلاط، ولكن على سبيل ظهور صورة الإنسان في المرآة والأجسام الصقيلة النقية عند مقابلتها من غير حلول صورة الإنسان في المرآة وكظهور نقش الخاتم وكل طابع في الشمع والطين وكل ذي قابل للطبع من الأجسام من غير حلول نقش الخاتم والرسم في الشمع والطين والتراب. وقال بعضهم : أقول إن الكلمة اتحدت بجسد المسيح عليه السلام على معنى أنها حلته من غير مماسة ولا ممازجة ولا مخالطة كما أقول أن الله سبحانه الحال في السماء وليس بمماس لها ولا مخالط.
Discussion against them concerning the meaning of union. And their expressions differed about the meaning of union; some of them said: the meaning of union is that the Word, which is the Son, indwelled the body of Jesus, peace be upon him. And another group, namely the Jacobites and many of them, said: union is mixture and blending, and the Jacobites claimed that the Word of God turned into flesh and blood through the union. And many of them, I mean the Jacobites and Nestorians, claimed that the union of the Word with the humanity (nāsūt) is mixture and blending like the mixture and blending of water with wine and milk when poured into them and mixed with them. And some of them claimed that the meaning of the union of the Word with the humanity, which is the body, is that it took it as a temple and a locus, and its management of things upon it and its manifestation in it to the exclusion of others. And they differed over the meaning of the manifestation of the Word in the temple and its putting it on and showing management through it. Most of them said: its meaning is that it indwelled it, mingled with it, and mixed with it like the mixing of wine and milk with water when they blend. And some of them said: the manifestation of the Word in the body and its union with it is not in the sense of mixture and blending, but in the manner of the appearance of a human image in a mirror and in polished, pure bodies when facing them, without the human image indwelling the mirror, and like the appearance of the impression of a seal and every stamp in wax, clay, and every body capable of receiving an impression, without the impression of the seal and the design indwelling the wax, clay, and dust. And some of them said: I say that the Word united with the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, in the sense that it indwelled it without touching, without mixing, and without blending, just as I say that God, glory be to Him, is the one who indwells heaven without being in contact with it or mixing with it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of various Christian views on the union (ittiḥād) of the Divine Word (the Son) with the human body of Christ. Baqillani presents multiple Christian positions: (1) Some say the Word simply indwelled the body. (2) The Jacobites and others say it is a physical mixture, like wine mixed with water, even claiming the Word turned into flesh. (3) The Nestorians also hold a mixture view. (4) Some say the body is a temple or instrument through which the Word acts. Among those who speak of manifestation, most hold to indwelling and mingling, while a minority compare it to an image in a mirror or an impression on wax—without actual indwelling. Finally, one group says the Word indwells the body without contact, mixing, or blending, analogous to God being 'in heaven' without spatial contact. Baqillani implies that these views are mutually contradictory and all problematic: indwelling implies divine limitations, mixing implies change, and analogies break down. The passage sets up the argument for Tawhid by exposing the incoherence of Christian formulations.

Key Terms
اتحاد
كلمة
ناسوت
هيكل
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Christian group claimed that the Word of God turned into flesh and blood through the union?
A) The Jacobites
B) The Nestorians
C) Those who held the instrumental view
D) All Christians

Key Takeaway
Christian views on the union of the Word with Christ range from simple indwelling to physical mixture and instrumental indwelling, none of which are consistent with Islamic tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in your own thoughts about Allah, slipped into imagining that He 'enters' or 'dwells in' something in creation—like your heart, a place, or a thing? How does this passage expose why such a thought is not only wrong but a direct echo of flawed Christian doctrines? What will you do today to guard your heart from such subtle shirk?
?
The Christians in this passage hold contradictory views about their most fundamental belief—the union between divine and human. Do you know the details of your own aqeedah well enough to defend it if someone asked you? Are you satisfied with simply knowing the 'right answer', or are you actively studying to understand why these details are necessary to preserve tawhid? Write down one specific gap in your knowledge that you will fill this week.
?
When you read that some Christians claimed the Word literally turned into flesh and blood, you may feel a sense of superiority. But examine yourself: in what area of your life do you act as if Allah's commands can be 'mixed' with societal norms or your own desires—like mixing water with wine? This passage warns against any view that compromises divine transcendence. Where is your own compromise? What is one concrete step you will take today to separate clear obedience from worldly mixture?

Disagreements on Manifestation: Mirror vs. Non-Physical Indwelling
Baqillani reports disagreements on the nature of the Word's manifestation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreements among Christians on how the Word manifests in Christ's body, including the mirror analogy and the concept of indwelling without contact.

Core Idea
The manifestation of the Word is contested: is it like a mirror image or a non-spatial indwelling?

After listing the main views on the union, Baqillani turns to the specific issue of how the Word manifests in the body. He reports a sharp disagreement: the majority hold to literal indwelling and mingling, while a minority propose analogies from mirrors and seals. A final group even claims indwelling without physical contact, comparing it to God's presence in heaven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on manifestation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Focus on the different analogies used to explain the manifestation.
The key terms are ظهور, حلول, مماسة, ممازجة.
2
Identify the majority view
Most Christians said the manifestation means the Word indwells, mingles, and mixes with the body, like wine and milk mixing with water.
This is essentially the same as the mixture view.
3
Understand the mirror analogy
Some Christians rejected mixture and compared the appearance to an image in a mirror or an impression on wax, where the image does not literally indwell the object.
This avoids physical mixture but still implies some form of presence.
4
Examine the non-contact indwelling view
A final group said the Word indwells without touching, mixing, or blending, analogous to God's indwelling of heaven without physical contact.
This view attempts to preserve transcendence while maintaining indwelling.
5
Reflect on the contradictions
Consider why these different analogies undermine each other. Baqillani implies that all are problematic because they either imply change or spatial limitation.
This lesson sets the stage for Baqillani's argument for divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Discussion against them concerning the meaning of union. And their expressions differed about the meaning of union; some of them said: the meaning of union is that the Word, which is the Son, indwelled the body of Jesus, peace be upon him. And another group, namely the Jacobites and many of them, said: union is mixture and blending, and the Jacobites claimed that the Word of God turned into flesh and blood through the union. And many of them, I mean the Jacobites and Nestorians, claimed that the union of the Word with the humanity (nāsūt) is mixture and blending like the mixture and blending of water with wine and milk when poured into them and mixed with them. And some of them claimed that the meaning of the union of the Word with the humanity, which is the body, is that it took it as a temple and a locus, and its management of things upon it and its manifestation in it to the exclusion of others. And they differed over the meaning of the manifestation of the Word in the temple and its putting it on and showing management through it. Most of them said: its meaning is that it indwelled it, mingled with it, and mixed with it like the mixing of wine and milk with water when they blend. And some of them said: the manifestation of the Word in the body and its union with it is not in the sense of mixture and blending, but in the manner of the appearance of a human image in a mirror and in polished, pure bodies when facing them, without the human image indwelling the mirror, and like the appearance of the impression of a seal and every stamp in wax, clay, and every body capable of receiving an impression, without the impression of the seal and the design indwelling the wax, clay, and dust. And some of them said: I say that the Word united with the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, in the sense that it indwelled it without touching, without mixing, and without blending, just as I say that God, glory be to Him, is the one who indwells heaven without being in contact with it or mixing with it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of various Christian views on the union (ittiḥād) of the Divine Word (the Son) with the human body of Christ. Baqillani presents multiple Christian positions: (1) Some say the Word simply indwelled the body. (2) The Jacobites and others say it is a physical mixture, like wine mixed with water, even claiming the Word turned into flesh. (3) The Nestorians also hold a mixture view. (4) Some say the body is a temple or instrument through which the Word acts. Among those who speak of manifestation, most hold to indwelling and mingling, while a minority compare it to an image in a mirror or an impression on wax—without actual indwelling. Finally, one group says the Word indwells the body without contact, mixing, or blending, analogous to God being 'in heaven' without spatial contact. Baqillani implies that these views are mutually contradictory and all problematic: indwelling implies divine limitations, mixing implies change, and analogies break down. The passage sets up the argument for Tawhid by exposing the incoherence of Christian formulations.

Key Terms
ظهور
حلول
مماسة
ممازجة
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the minority view in the passage, how is the manifestation of the Word in the body compared?
A) Like water mixing with wine
B) Like a human image in a mirror
C) Like God indwelling heaven without contact
D) Like the Word turning into flesh

Key Takeaway
Christian explanations of the Word's manifestation range from literal mixing to mirror-like appearance and non-contact indwelling, revealing deep inconsistencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on how God relates to His creation, do you ever unknowingly adopt analogies that reduce His transcendence? Which analogy from this passage (mixture, mirror, or non-contact indwelling) do you find yourself most tempted to apply to God, and why does this lesson expose the danger in it?
?
Baqillani reports that Christian explanations are contradictory. Have you ever held two contradictory beliefs about God without realizing it? What specific contradiction in your own understanding might this passage reveal if you honestly examine it?
?
The third group compares the Word's indwelling to God's presence in heaven: 'without contact or mixing'. Do you truly believe that God is present everywhere without being confined? Or do you have a mental image of God in a specific place? This lesson challenges you to purify your concept of God's presence. Will you commit to removing any spatial or physical notion from your belief tonight?

Christian Conceptions of Union as Mixture
Baqillani reports Jacobite and Nestorian conceptions of the union as mixture and amalgamation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various Christian views reported by Baqillani on how the divine Word united with the human nature of Jesus.

Core Idea
Baqillani catalogues Christian doctrines of union as mixture, transformation, and indwelling.

This passage from Baqillani’s Tamhid is a straightforward report of Christian theological positions on the Incarnation. He lists several ways in which the Jacobites and Nestorians understood the union of the divine Word with the human body. Understanding these views is essential for grasping the Islamic refutations that follow later in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage slowly, noting the different descriptions of the union (ittihad).
Pay attention to the specific Arabic terms for mixture, amalgamation, temple, and locus.
2
Identify the sects and their views
The passage mentions two groups: Jacobites and Nestorians. Underline the views attributed to each.
3
Compare the three descriptions
There are three distinct descriptions: literal mixture, transformation into flesh, and indwelling as a temple. Notice the progression.
The third description (temple) is attributed to 'some among them' and is a more refined view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت طائفة أخرى وهم اليعاقبة وكثير منهم : إن الاتحاد هو اختلاط وامتزاج، وزعمت اليعقوبية أن كلمة الله انقلبت لحماً ودماً بالاتحاد. وزعم كثير منهم، أعني اليعقوبية والنسطورية أن اتحاد الكلمة بالناسوت اختلاط وامتزاج كاختلاط الماء وامتزاجه بالخمر واللبن إذا صُبّ فيهما ومزج بهما. وزعم قوم منهم أن معنى اتحاد الكلمة بالناسوت الذي هو الجسد هو اتحادها له هيكلاً ومحلاً وتدبيرها الأشياء عليه وظهورها فيه دون غيره.
And another sect, the Jacobites, and many of them said that the union is mixture and amalgamation. The Jacobites claimed that the Word of God turned into flesh and blood through the union. And many of them, namely the Jacobites and Nestorians, claimed that the union of the Word with the human nature (al-nasut) is mixture and amalgamation like the mixing and amalgamation of water with wine and milk when it is poured into them and mixed with them. And some among them claimed that the meaning of the union of the Word with the human nature, which is the body, is that it united with it as a temple (haykal) and a locus (mahall), and its management of things upon it and its appearing in it to the exclusion of others.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a report of Christian doctrinal positions on the union (ittihad) of the divine Word (al-kalima) with the human nature (al-nasut) of Jesus. Baqillani first presents the view of the Jacobites (al-Ya'qubiyya) and many among them that the union is a literal mixture and amalgamation (ikhtilat wa imtizaj). They claim that the Word of God turned into flesh and blood. He then mentions that many of them, including both Jacobites and Nestorians, specify that this union is like the mixing of water with wine or milk when poured together. Finally, he notes that some among them hold that the union means the Word took the body as a temple (haykal) and abode (mahall), managing affairs through it and appearing in it alone. The passage is purely descriptive, setting up the views that Baqillani will later refute.

النسطورية (al-Nusituriya)

امتزاج (imtizaj)
هيكل (haykal)
محل (mahall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Muslims believe in a literal union of God with a body.
Correction: Baqillani is reporting Christian views, not endorsing them. Islamic theology rejects any literal union or incarnation.

Checkpoint
How did the Jacobites and Nestorians describe the union of the Word with the human nature?

B) As a mixture and amalgamation like water with wine or milk

Key Takeaway
Baqillani reports that Jacobites and Nestorians understood the Incarnation as a mixture, transformation, or indwelling, each a different model of divine-human union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Jacobites believed the Word literally turned into flesh and blood, do you feel any discomfort in your heart? Have you ever, even subtly, imagined God as being 'mixed' with the world or with a human? What would it mean for your worship if you did?
?
The Nestorians and Jacobites used analogies like water mixed with wine to explain the union. Think about the analogies you use when describing God to yourself or others. Do any of those analogies diminish His transcendence? How can you train your mind to avoid such pitfalls?

Two Christian Views on the Word's Embodiment
Baqillani reports two Christian interpretations on the Word's manifestation in the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Christian interpretations of the Word's manifestation in the body (mixture vs. non-physical appearance) as reported by Baqillani, and why this sets up a refutation.

Core Idea
Christians disagreed: does the Word mix with the body or merely appear like a mirror image?

In this passage, Baqillani presents a sectarian disagreement among Christians regarding how the Word (al-kalima) appears in the body (haykal). The majority held a literal mixture, while a minority denied physical mixing and offered analogies of mirror images and seal imprints. Understanding these views is essential for the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the two positions.
Notice the key terms like 'halla', 'mazaja', 'ikhtalata' for the majority view, and 'zuhur' for the minority.
2
Identify the two groups and their positions
Find the majority view (aktharuhum) and the minority view (qawm minhum). Compare their definitions of 'zuhur'.
The majority says the Word indwelt, mixed, and blended with the body. The minority denies this blending.
3
Examine the analogies of the minority
The minority uses two analogies: the image in a mirror and the seal imprint. Note that both emphasize appearance without indwelling.
The mirror analogy: the person's image appears in the mirror without the image dwelling in it. The seal analogy: the imprint appears in wax without the seal's engraving transferring.
4
Reflect on Baqillani's purpose
Ask yourself why Baqillani reports these views. He is setting up a refutation of both positions later in the text.
Understanding the views helps you see the foundation for the theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في معنى ظهور الكلمة في الهيكل وادّراعها له وإظهار التدبير عليه، فقال أكثرهم : معنى ذلك أنها حلّته ومازجته واختلطت به اختلاط الخمر واللبن بالماء عند امتزاجهما. وقال قوم منهم : إن ظهور الكلمة في الجسد واتحادها به ليس على معنى المزاج والاختلاط، ولكن على سبيل ظهور صورة الإنسان في المرآة والأجسام الصقيلة النقية عند مقابلتها من غير حلول صورة الإنسان في المرآة، وكظهور نقش الخاتم وكل طابع في الشمع والطين وكل ذي قابل للطبع من الأجسام من غير حلول نقش الخاتم والرسم في الشمع والطين والتراب.
And they disagreed on the meaning of the appearing of the Word in the temple (haykal) and its donning of it and the manifestation of management upon it. The majority of them said: the meaning of that is that it indwelt it, mixed with it, and blended with it, like the mixing of wine and milk with water when they are mingled. And a group among them said: the appearing of the Word in the body and its union with it is not on the meaning of blending and mixture, but on the way of the appearing of the image of a person in a mirror and in polished, pure bodies when facing them, without the indwelling of the person's image in the mirror; and like the appearing of the engraving of the seal (khatam) and every stamp (tabi') in wax, clay, and every body capable of receiving an impression, without the indwelling of the engraving of the seal and the design in the wax, clay, and dirt.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage reports a disagreement among Christians about how the Word (al-kalima) appears in the body (the 'temple' or 'armor'). The majority hold that it is a real indwelling (halla), mixing (mazaja), and blending (ikhtalat) like wine and milk with water. A group, however, denies this physical mixing and instead offers two analogies: (1) the appearance of a person's image in a mirror or polished surface, where the image does not actually dwell in the mirror; (2) the imprint of a seal on wax or clay, where the seal's engraving does not transfer into the material. These analogies attempt to explain the union without attributing physical mixture to the divine. Baqillani is setting up these views for later refutation.

Key Terms
ظهور (zuhur)
هيكل (haykal)
درع (dir' — donning)
مرآة (mir'at)
نقش (naqsh)
خاتم (khatam)
طابع (tabi')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians believed the Word physically mixed with the body.
Correction: A group denied physical mixture and used analogies of mirror images and seal imprints to explain the union, without literal indwelling.

Checkpoint
According to the minority view, how does the appearing of the Word in the body resemble a mirror?
A) The image actually dwells in the mirror
B) The image appears without indwelling
C) The mirror becomes the person
D) The image is a physical part of the mirror

Key Takeaway
Baqillani reports two Christian views on the Word's embodiment: literal mixing vs. non-physical appearance, setting the stage for refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Christians used analogies to explain their belief. Are there any beliefs you hold that you try to make more palatable with analogies, even if those analogies compromise the truth? Will you examine them today?
?
Baqillani recorded views he knew were wrong. Do you take time to understand false beliefs before rejecting them, or do you dismiss them without empathy? How can you better engage with those who disagree with you?
?
The majority view described mixing like wine and water—a physical blending. If you believe God's speech (the Quran) is uncreated, what does that imply about how it relates to creation? Can you accept that it is not mixed with creation, yet still acts upon it?

Christian View of Non-Contact Indwelling
Baqillani reports a Christian view of non-contact indwelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some Christians described the union of the Word and Christ's body as a non-physical indwelling, using analogies of God in heaven and intellect in soul.

Core Idea
Not all Christians believed in a physical union; some described it as God's indwelling without contact.

Baqillani presents a third Christian explanation of the incarnation that tries to avoid materialism. These Christians explicitly deny any physical contact, mixing, or mingling. Instead, they compare the union to two familiar examples: God's presence in heaven and the intellect's presence in the soul. This passage shows that some Christians sought to preserve divine transcendence within the union.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three terms denied: mumassa (touching), mumazaja (mingling), mukhalata (mixing). Then see the two analogies.
These terms are key to understanding the non-physical nature of the union.
2
Identify the analogies
The author gives two comparisons: God's indwelling of heaven, and the intellect's indwelling of the soul. Both are said to occur without contact or mixing.
The second analogy uses 'aql (intellect) as a substance (jawhar) indwelling the soul.
3
Reflect on the theological motive
Ask yourself why these Christians would choose these analogies. What problem are they trying to solve? They want to affirm real presence without reducing God to a physical entity.
This shows the diversity of Christian attempts to explain the incarnation.
4
Consider Baqillani's presentation
Note that Baqillani is reporting this view as part of his refutation. He does not accept it, but he presents it fairly. Think about how this view might be vulnerable to critique.
Baqillani's own position becomes clearer in the context of the larger work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : أقول إن الكلمة اتحدت بجسد المسيح عليه السلام على معنى أنها حلته من غير مماسة ولا ممازجة ولا مخالطة كما أقول أن الله سبحانه الحال في السماء وليس بمماس لها ولا مخالط لها، وكما أقول : إن العقل جوهر للنفس حال في النفس وهو مع ذلك غير مخالط ولا مماس لها.
And some of them said: I say that the Word united with the body of the Messiah, peace be upon him, on the meaning that it indwelt it without touching (mumassa), without mingling (mumazaja), and without mixing (mukhalata), just as I say that God, exalted, is the indweller of heaven without being in contact with it or mixing with it, and just as I say that the intellect is a substance (jawhar) for the soul, indwelling in the soul, and yet it is not mixing with it nor in contact with it.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a third Christian view on the nature of the union. Some Christians explicitly reject physical contact (mumassa), mingling (mumazaja), and mixing (mukhalata). They instead compare the union to two cases: (1) God's relation to heaven — God is described as 'dwelling' (hal) in heaven without being in physical contact with it; (2) the intellect's relation to the soul — the intellect is a substance (jawhar) that indwells (hal) in the soul without mixing or contact. This view attempts to preserve divine transcendence by denying any materialistic union, while still affirming a real presence. Baqillani reports this as part of Christian attempts to explain the incarnation.

Key Terms
مماسة (mumassa)
ممازجة (mumazaja)
مخالطة (mukhalata)
حال (hal — indwelling)
عقل ('aql)
جوهر (jawhar)
نفس (nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians believed the union of the Word and Christ's body was a literal, physical mixture.
Correction: This passage shows that some Christians explicitly denied physical contact, mingling, and mixing, describing the union instead as a non-physical indwelling similar to God's presence in heaven or the intellect's presence in the soul.

Checkpoint

A) A mixture of water and milk
B) A face in a mirror
C) God's indwelling of heaven or the intellect's indwelling of the soul without contact
D) The impression of a seal on wax

Key Takeaway
Some Christians described the incarnation as a non-physical indwelling, using analogies of God in heaven and intellect in soul to deny any material contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's presence in your life, do you ever imagine Him being 'in' you or 'with' you in a way that feels physical or spatial? How does the Christian view of non-contact indwelling challenge you to refine that mental image? This week, spend five minutes silently reflecting on God's transcendence—ask yourself: Do I truly worship a God who is beyond all physical contact?
?
The Christian speaker uses analogies (God in heaven, intellect in soul) to describe something he admits is mysterious. What analogies do you rely on to understand Allah? Are you willing to let go of any analogy if it leads to false implications? Before sleeping tonight, identify one analogy you hold about Allah and test it against the Qur'anic principle: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11).
?
Baqillani reports this view without immediate refutation, allowing it to speak for itself. How often do you honestly examine opposing viewpoints before dismissing them? Do you fear that understanding another's perspective might weaken your faith or strengthen it? This week, choose one belief you disagree with and read its own best articulation—then ask: Is my rejection rooted in evidence or in comfort?

The Melkite View: Two Become One Numerically
Baqillani reports the Melkite (Royal) view that the union makes two into one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Melkite Christian claim that the union (ittihad) makes the Word and body numerically one, in contrast to other views.

Core Idea
The Melkites claimed that union made two entities become one numerical reality.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani reports a specific Christian sect's view on the union of the Word and body. The Melkites (or 'Royalists') held a strong ontological claim: the two become one, multiplicity becomes fewness. This lesson unpacks that claim and its significance in the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its phrasing. Notice the key terms: الروم, الملكية, اثنان, واحد.
The passage is a single sentence summarizing the Melkite doctrine.
2
Understand the translation
Read the literal English translation. Pay attention to how 'two became one' and 'multiplicity became fewness' are expressed.
3
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms for 'two' (اثنان), 'one' (واحد), 'multiplicity' (كثرة), and 'fewness' (قلة). Understand their role in the claim.
These terms are central to the Melkite assertion of numerical unity.
4
Consider the contrast
Think about how this view differs from other Christian views (e.g., mixture or indwelling). The Melkite claim is about a literal, ontological change.
Baqillani presents this as the well-known doctrine among them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت الروم وهي الملكية أن معنى اتحاد الكلمة بالجسد أن الاثنين صارا واحداً وصارت الكثرة قلة وصارت الكلمة وما اتحدت به واحداً وكان هذا الواحد بالاتحاد اثنين قبل ذلك هذا جملة المشهور عنهم في معنى الاتحاد.
And the Romans (al-Rum), who are the Melkites (al-Malikiyya), claimed that the meaning of the union of the Word with the body is that the two became one, and multiplicity became fewness, and the Word and that with which it united became one, and this one by union was two before that. This is the summary of what is well-known from them regarding the meaning of the union.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage describes the Melkite (or 'Royal') Christian view of the union. The Melkites assert that through the union, the two (the Word and the body) become numerically one (wahid). Multiplicity (kathra) is reduced to fewness (qilla). The Word and what it united with become a single entity, whereas before the union they were two. Baqillani presents this as the well-known (mashhur) doctrine among them. This view emphasizes a real ontological unity, contrasting with the earlier views that spoke of mixture or indwelling without change.

Key Terms
الروم (al-Rum)
الملكية (al-Malikiyya)
اثنان (ithnan)
واحد (wahid)
كثرة (kathra)
قلة (qilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Christians believed in a mere moral union; the Melkites believed in a literal numerical unity.
Correction: The Melkites held a literal ontological union where two become one numerically, not merely a moral or accidental connection.

Checkpoint
What is the Melkite claim about the outcome of the union?
A) The Word and body remain two distinct substances
B) The two become one, and multiplicity becomes fewness
C) The Word is only metaphorically united
D) The body ceases to exist

Key Takeaway
The Melkites taught that the Word and body become one numerical entity through union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Christians themselves disagreed on the nature of Jesus. Have you ever assumed that all Christians believe the same thing about Christ? How does this challenge you to be more precise in understanding your own beliefs and those of others? Will you commit to seeking accurate information before forming judgments?
?
Baqillani presents the Melkite view without refuting it in this passage. When you hear a belief that seems contradictory (like two becoming one), do you quickly dismiss it or do you strive to understand its internal logic first? What does your reaction reveal about your intellectual humility?
?
The Melkites claimed that multiplicity became fewness. In your own spiritual life, are you distracted by many concerns instead of focusing on the One? How can this lesson spur you to reduce 'kathra' and seek 'qilla' in your worship of Allah alone?

Refuting the Mirror Analogy for Union
Baqillani refutes the mirror analogy for union via illusion of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the mirror analogy for the Christian union by analyzing his explanation of mirror perception as an illusion based on custom and reflection.

Core Idea
Mirrors don't contain images; the analogy for union is therefore baseless.

Christian theologians sometimes used the analogy of a face appearing in a mirror to explain how the Word could unite with a human body without mixing. In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani dismantles this analogy by showing that nothing actually appears in a mirror—our perception is a mental event caused by light reflection, not a real image. Understanding his argument will sharpen your ability to critique theological analogies that rely on mistaken physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the claim being refuted and the reasoning Baqillani uses to reject it.
The passage begins with 'As for those among them'—this signals a refutation of a specific sectarian view.
2
Identify the Christian claim
Pinpoint exactly what the Christian analogy asserts: that the union is like a face appearing in a mirror without the face entering the mirror.
Baqillani calls this claim 'meaningless' (la ma'na lahu).
3
Analyze Baqillani's explanation of mirror perception
Note his alternative explanation: no face, image, or engraving actually enters the mirror. What we perceive is a self-perception caused by the reflection of rays and governed by habitual custom (jary al-'adah).
He references the mutakallimun who held this theory of reflection.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since nothing real appears in the mirror, the analogy collapses. The union cannot be built on a false premise.
Baqillani says he has explained this fully elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من زعم منهم أن معنى الاتحاد هو ظهور الابن في الجسد وادراعه له على سبيل ظهور الوجه في المرآة والنقش في المطبوع من غير حلول الوجه في المرآة وانتقال النقش إلى الشمع، فلا معنى له؛ لأن الوجه ليس يظهر في المرآة ولا صورة مثله، ولا ينتقل إليها ولا يوجد على صفحتها ولا ممازجاً لها وإنما يدرك الإنسان وجه نفسه عند مقابلة الأجسام الصافية الصقيلة بإدراك يحدث له بجري العادة عند مقابلة الأجسام بانعكاس الشعاع على ما يذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين، فيظن عند إدراكه لنفسه ومقابلة الجسم الصقيل أن في المرآة صورة هي وجه أو مثل وجه، وليس كذلك؛ وقد بينا هذا في غير هذا الموضع بما يغني الناظر فيه وإذا ثبت أنه لا شيء يظهر في المرآة ولا يختص بها بطل بناء الاتحاد عليه.
As for those among them who claim that the meaning of the union is the appearing of the Son in the body and his donning of it in the manner of the appearing of a face in a mirror and the engraving in the imprinted object, without the indwelling of the face in the mirror and the transfer of the engraving to the wax, it is meaningless. For the face does not appear in the mirror, nor does its image, nor does it transfer to it, nor does it exist on its surface nor as something mingled with it. Rather, a person perceives his own face when facing pure, polished bodies through a perception that occurs to him by the custom (jary al-‘adah) when facing bodies by the reflection (in‘ikas) of rays (shu‘a‘), according to what some theologians (mutakallimun) hold. So he imagines, when perceiving himself and facing the polished body, that in the mirror there is an image that is a face or like a face, but it is not so. We have explained this elsewhere in a way that suffices the examiner. And since it is established that nothing appears in the mirror and that nothing is particular to it, the construction of the union upon it becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of the Christian analogy that the union of the Word with the body is like a face appearing in a mirror. Baqillani argues that this analogy is meaningless (la ma‘na lahu) because, in reality, no face or image actually appears in the mirror. He explains that perception of one's own face when looking at a polished surface is a psychological event caused by the reflection of rays, not by an actual presence of an image in the mirror. People mistakenly imagine there is an image, but there is not. Since nothing real appears in the mirror, using this as an analogy for the union fails. Baqillani mentions he has fully explained this elsewhere. Thus, the union cannot be built on such a false analogy.

Key Terms
ظهور الوجه (zuhur al-wajh)
مرآة (mir'at)
انعكاس (in'ikas)
شعاع (shu'a')
مطبوع (matbu')
جري العادة (jary al-'adah)
متكلمون (mutakallimun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Christians believe the union of the Word with the body is like a face appearing in a mirror—a real image present without physical indwelling.
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that no image actually exists in the mirror; the perception is an illusion caused by reflection of rays and custom. Thus the analogy is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is Baqillani's main reason for rejecting the mirror analogy?
A) The union is too holy to be compared to a mirror
B) Nothing actually appears in the mirror; perception is an illusion due to custom and reflection
C) The mirror is a physical object, but the union is spiritual
D) The face in the mirror is a different face

Key Takeaway
The mirror analogy for the union fails because mirrors contain no real images—only illusions—so the analogy has no basis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that a powerful analogy for union crumbles when examined. What analogy or metaphor do you rely on to understand God that might be based on a similar illusion? Are you willing to submit it to the same scrutiny Baqillani applied?
?
Baqillani shows that our senses can deceive us into believing something exists when it doesn't. In your spiritual journey, what 'image in the mirror' have you mistaken for truth? Will you accept the correction, even if it means losing a comforting belief?
?
The Christian analogy fails because its foundation—mirrors containing images—is false. What foundational assumptions in your own faith might be false? Can you honestly seek to dismantle them, trusting that truth remains when illusions are stripped away?

Baqillani Dismisses the Seal Imprint Analogy
Baqillani rejects the seal imprint analogy as false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the analogy of a seal's imprint in wax or clay as a model for the union of the Word with the body.

Core Idea
The seal imprint analogy is false; impression is not transference.

We now encounter the second Christian analogy for the union: comparing it to a seal's image appearing in wax or clay. Baqillani dismisses it in a single decisive sentence. In this short lesson, we examine why this analogy fails and what it reveals about the Christian claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note that Baqillani uses the word 'bāṭil' (false) to reject the analogy outright.
This is a concise dismissal; no lengthy rebuttal follows.
2
Identify the analogy
Recognize what the analogy claims: that just as a seal's engraving appears in wax without the seal moving into the wax, so the divine Word appears in the body of Jesus.
The analogy attempts to explain a 'presence without transference'.
3
Understand the refutation
Reflect on why Baqillani calls it false. The impression in wax is merely a shape, not the actual seal. Similarly, the analogy fails to prove a real union of the divine essence with the body.
Baqillani likely considers the analogy insufficient because it implies only a surface-level impression, not a true union.
4
Contrast with the mirror analogy
Compare this refutation with the earlier longer one about the mirror. Notice that here Baqillani does not elaborate, perhaps because the flaw is similar.
Both analogies involve images without substance, so the same critique applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for their likening of that to the appearing of the engraving of the impression (naqsh al-tab') in wax and clay, it is false (batil).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This short passage is a direct refutation of the second Christian analogy, namely comparing the union to the appearing of a seal's engraving in wax or clay. Baqillani dismisses it as simply false (batil). Unlike the preceding lengthy refutation of the mirror analogy, here he does not elaborate further within this passage, perhaps because the underlying issue is similar: no actual transference of the seal's form occurs; the wax merely receives an impression. The analogy fails to prove a real union of the Word with the body.

Refuting the Mirror Analogy for Union
Baqillani refutes the Christian mirror analogy for the union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the Christian analogy of a face in a mirror for the incarnation, and how he uses the physics of reflection to dismantle it.

Core Idea
The mirror analogy fails because the face never truly enters the mirror.

Baqillani engages with a sophisticated Christian argument that attempts to describe the union of the Word with the body by comparing it to the appearance of a face in a mirror. This passage is a clear example of rational debate between theologians. The author first states the Christian claim, then presents the specific analogy, and then thoroughly refutes it by analyzing the actual physical process of reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani presents the Christian view first before refuting it.
2
Identify the Christian claim and the analogy
The passage begins by summarizing the Melkite view of union, then presents a specific analogy: the appearance of the Son in the body is like a face in a mirror or an engraving in wax. Find these two parts.
The analogy attempts to avoid implying indwelling (hulul) or mixing.
3
Understand the refutation using mirror physics
Baqillani argues that nothing actually appears in the mirror; rather, a person perceives their own face via reflected light. He contrasts this with the idea of transfer or presence. Trace his reasoning step by step.
He uses the concept of reflection of rays (ins'ikāsh al-shu'ā') and habit (jary al-'ādah) to explain the perception.
4
Conclude why the analogy collapses
Since nothing is truly in the mirror, the analogy cannot support the doctrine of union. Baqillani's final statement makes this clear. Reflect on how this refutation demonstrates his method of rational critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت الروم وهي الملكية أن معنى اتحاد الكلمة بالجسد أن الاثنين صارا واحداً وصارت الكثرة قلة وصارت الكلمة وما اتحدت به واحداً وكان هذا الواحد بالاتحاد اثنين قبل ذلك هذا جملة المشهور عنهم في معنى الاتحاد. فأما من زعم منهم أن معنى الاتحاد هو ظهور الابن في الجسد وادراعه له على سبيل ظهور الوجه في المرآة والنقش في المطبوع من غير حلول الوجه في المرآة وانتقال النقش إلى الشمع، فلا معنى له؛ لأن الوجه ليس يظهر في المرآة ولا صورة مثله، ولا ينتقل إليها ولا يوجد على صفحتها ولا ممازجاً لها وإنما يدرك الإنسان وجه نفسه عند مقابلة الأجسام الصافية الصقيلة بإدراك يحدث له بجري العادة عند مقابلة الأجسام بانعكاس الشعاع على ما يذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين، فيظن عند إدراكه لنفسه ومقابلة الجسم الصقيل أن في المرآة صورة هي وجه أو مثل وجه، وليس كذلك؛ وقد بينا هذا في غير موضع بما يغني الناظر فيه وإذا ثبت أنه لا شيء يظهر في المرآة ولا يختص بها بطل بناء الاتحاد عليه
The Byzantines – who are the Melkites – claimed that the meaning of the union of the Word with the body is that the two become one, and multiplicity becomes fewness, and the Word and that with which it united become one, and this one by union was two before that. This is the summary of what is well-known from them concerning the meaning of union. As for those among them who claimed that the meaning of union is the appearance of the Son in the body and his clothing himself with it, in the manner of the appearance of the face in the mirror and the engraving in the stamped object, without the indwelling of the face in the mirror and the transfer of the engraving to the wax – this has no meaning; because the face does not appear in the mirror, nor does a form like it, nor does it transfer into it, nor does it exist on its surface, nor is it mixed with it. Rather, a person perceives his own face when facing smooth polished bodies, by a perception that occurs for him by habit when facing bodies, by the reflection of rays, according to what some theologians hold. So he supposes, when perceiving himself and facing a polished body, that in the mirror there is a form that is a face or like a face, but it is not so. We have explained this elsewhere in a way that suffices the observer. And when it is established that nothing appears in the mirror nor is specifically related to it, the construction of union upon it collapses.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian analogy of the face appearing in a mirror to explain the incarnation (ittihad). The author first states the Melkite Christian claim that union of the Word (al‑kalima) with the body makes the two one, reducing multiplicity. Then he addresses a specific subgroup who liken this union to the appearance of the Son in the body, analogous to a face in a mirror or an engraving in wax – claiming no actual indwelling. Baqillani rejects this entirely. He explains that the face does not truly appear in the mirror; rather, a person perceives their own face via reflected light due to habit, and only imagines a form there. No image actually enters or adheres to the mirror. Since nothing is truly in the mirror, the entire analogy is baseless and cannot support the doctrine of union. The passage proceeds in the author's own order: exposition of the Christian view, statement of the analogy, analysis of the mirror phenomenon, and conclusion that the analogy fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the face or its image literally enters or appears in the mirror.
Correction: Baqillani shows that only a perception of one's own face occurs via reflected light; no image actually resides in the mirror.

Baqillani refutes the Christian mirror analogy for union by demonstrating that the face does not truly appear in the mirror, thereby dismantling the basis for the analogy.

?
The mirror illusion is convincing but false. What about your own spiritual perception—could you be mistaking a reflection of truth for Truth itself? Ask God to tear away the illusions so you may see Him as He truly is, even if it costs you your cherished mental images.

Refuting the Analogy of the Seal Impression
Baqillani refutes the analogy of a seal impression in wax.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the Christian analogy of a seal impression leads to four hypostases rather than the Trinity.

Core Idea
The impression is not the stamp; confusion between them multiplies divine persons.

Baqillani presents a logical dismantling of a common Christian analogy used to explain the Incarnation. He shows that if the Word appears in Jesus like a seal impression, it implies a separate entity, leading to two words and two sons, i.e., four hypostases. This passage highlights the importance of accurate conceptual distinctions in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Baqillani's argument against the analogy.
Notice the logical structure: exposition, analysis, conclusion.
2
Identify the analogy and its flaw
The Christians liken the Word's presence in Jesus to a stamp's impression in wax. Baqillani points out that the impression is not the stamp itself.
The letters in wax are part of wax, not part of the stamp.
3
Trace the theological consequence
If the impression is other than the stamp, then what appears in Jesus is other than the Word. This leads to two words and two sons, i.e., four hypostases.
This contradicts the Trinity, which claims three hypostases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تشبيههم ذلك بظهور نقش الطبع في الشمع والطين فإنه باطل وتخليط من قائله؛ وذلك أن الظاهر في الشمع شيء مثل نقش الخاتم، وهو غيره، لأن الحروف الموجودة بالشمع هي بعض له وجزء من أجزائه؛ وما في الطابع من الحروف هو بعض الطابع ومن جملته وهما غيران يصح وجود مع عدم الآخر، فظنهم أن نفس النقش الذي في الشمع هو نفس الطابع جهل وتخليط فيجب على هذا إن لم تكن الكلمة نفسها هي الظاهرة في جسد المسيح أن يكون الظاهر فيه غيرها وهو شيء مثلها، وأن يكون الله سبحانه ابنان وكلمتان، أحدهما لا يحل الأجسام ولا يتخذها هيكلاً ومكاناً، والآخر حال في جسد المسيح، وهذا قول بأربعة أقانيم وترك القول بالتثليث.
As for their likening that to the appearance of the impression of the stamp in wax and clay, it is false and confused talk from its speaker. That is because what appears in the wax is something like the engraving of the seal ring, and it is other than it, for the letters present in the wax are some of it and a part of its parts; while what is in the stamp of letters is some of the stamp and part of it, and they are two different things, the existence of one being valid without the other. So their supposition that the very impression which is in the wax is the same as the stamp is ignorance and confusion. It follows from this that if the Word itself is not what appears in the body of the Messiah, then what appears in it is other than it, and it is something like it, and God – exalted be He – would have two sons and two words: one does not dwell in bodies nor take them as a temple and place, and the other is indwelling in the body of the Messiah. And this is a statement of four hypostases and an abandonment of the statement of the Trinity.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian analogy of a stamp's impression in wax or clay. Baqillani argues that the impression in wax is not identical to the stamp itself; it is a separate thing. The letters in the wax are part of the wax, while the letters on the stamp are part of the stamp. They can exist without each other. Therefore, if Christians claim that the Word (al‑kalima) appears in Jesus' body like an impression, it would mean that the appearance is something other than the Word, leading to two sons and two words: one that does not indwell bodies, and one that does. This would mean four hypostases (aqanim), abandoning the Trinity. The passage follows a logical structure: exposition of the analogy, analysis showing the impression is not the stamp, then the theological consequence of multiple divine persons.

نقش
طابع
خاتم
أقانيم
تثليث
كلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impression in wax shares identity with the stamp.
Correction: Baqillani shows they are distinct: the impression is part of the wax, the stamp is a separate object.

Checkpoint

A) The Word and Jesus become one substance.
B) God would have two sons and two words, leading to four hypostases.
C) The Word is identical to Jesus.
D) The Trinity is affirmed.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the seal analogy logically leads to four hypostases, undermining the Christian claim of the Trinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an analogy to explain a difficult concept about God, only to realize later that it led to a misunderstanding? How does Baqillani's precise dissection challenge you to be more careful in your own thinking about divine matters? Will you commit to examining your own analogies for hidden errors?
?
This passage warns that confusing the impression with the stamp multiplies divine persons. In your own life, do you sometimes conflate God with His creation? ?

The Seal Analogy Debunked: Why the Word Cannot Be Identical to the Flesh
Baqillani refutes the Christian analogy of a seal impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the Christian analogy of a seal impression on wax as a model for the incarnation, and why it leads to absurdities like two sons of God.

Core Idea
The seal and its impression are distinct; likewise, the Word and Christ's body cannot be identical.

Baqillani begins his refutation by exposing a logical flaw in a common Christian analogy. Christians compared the incarnation to a seal imprinting its shape on wax. Baqillani shows that this analogy actually undermines their doctrine, because the imprint is part of the wax, not the seal itself. This leads to a cascade of theological absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani analyzes the seal-wax analogy in detail.
Focus on the distinction between the seal and the impression.
2
Identify the key claim
What is the Christian claim that Baqillani refutes? He shows that the imprint is not the seal itself.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Baqillani shows that if the Word is not identical to what appears in Christ, then there would be two sons and two words, implying four hypostases instead of three.
Consider how this argument uses logic to expose internal contradictions.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Reflect on why this refutation is important for defending divine unity (tawhid) and rejecting polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما وتخليط من قائله؛ وذلك أن الظاهر في الشمع شيء مثل نقش الخاتم، وهو غيره، لأن الحروف الموجودة بالشمع هي بعض له وجزء من أجزائه؛ وما في الطابع من الحروف هو بعض الطابع ومن جملته وهما غيران يصح وجود مع عدم الآخر، فظنهم أن نفس النقش الذي في الشمع هو نفس الطابع جهل وتخليط فيجب على هذا إن لم تكن الكلمة نفسها هي الظاهرة في جسد المسيح أن يكون الظاهر فيه غيرها وهو شيء مثلها، وأن يكون الله سبحانه ابنان وكلمتان، أحدهما لا يحل الأجسام ولا يتخذها هيكلاً ومكاناً، والآخر حال في جسد المسيح، وهذا قول بأربعة أقانيم وترك القول بالتثليث. وأما من قال إن الاتحاد إنما هو حلول الكلمة في المتحد به واختلاطها به وممازجتها له، فإنه يقال له: إذا جاز على الكلمة الحلول في الجسد المخلوق وممازجتها له واختلاطها به، وهي مع ذلك قديمة، فما أنكرتم من اجتماعها مع الجسد ومماستها له؟ وإذا جاز على القديم سبحانه المماسة والمجاورة والمخالطة للمحدث والممازجة له فلم لا يجوز عليه مقابلة المحدث ومحاذاته؟ ولم لا يجوز عليه الظهور والكمون والحركة والبعد والقرب والشغل، والتفريغ والتصوير والتركيب؟ فإن راموا في ذلك فصلاً لم يجدوه وإن مروا على ذلك قيل لهم: فإذا جاز أن يكون ما هذه صفته قديماً وقد كان في القدم غير مماس ولا ممازج ولا مخالط، فما أنكرتم أن يكون سائر الأجسام المماسة المختلطة المتحركة الساكنة قديمة؟ وما الذي جعل الكلمة التي هذه صفتها بالقدم أولى منها بالحدث؟ وما الذي جعل الأجسام بالحدث أولى من الكلمة؟ ويقال لليعقوبية: إذا جاز أن ينقلب ما ليس بلحم ولا دم لنفسه وما هو مخالف للدم واللحم لنفسه، لحماً ودماً بالاتحاد، فلم لا يجوز أن تنقلب الكلمة، التي تخالف المحدثات لنفسها وليست بمحدثة لنفسها محدثة بالاتحاد، فيصير القديم لنفسه بالاتحاد محدثاً عند اتحاده كما صار لحماً ودماً عند اتحاده، ولم لا يصير المحدث لنفسه قبل اتحاد القديم به قديماً عند اتحاد القديم به المتحد به فتصير الطبيعتان واحدة ويصير ما ليس بلحم ودم لحماً ودماً وما هو لحم ودم غير لحم ولا دم؟ فلا يجدون إلى دفع ذلك سبيلاً. فيخرج عن أن يكون لحماً ودماً عند اتحاده في وأما قول من قال إن الاتحاد هو حلول الكلمة في الناسوت من غير مماسة، وأنه كحلول الباري سبحانه في السماء وكحلوله على العرش من غير مماسة لهما، فإنه باطل غير معقول؛ وذلك أن الباري سبحانه ليس في السماء ولا هو مستو على عرشه بمعنى حلوله على العرش؛ لأنه لو كان حالاً في أحدهما ومستوياً على الآخر، بمعنى الحلول لوجب أن يكون مماساً لهما لا محالة. وأما قولهم إن العقل جوهر حال في النفس مماس لها فإنه باطل لأن الجوهر لا يحل في العرض وإنما يحل في الجسم على معنى المماسة له والاعتماد عليه واتخاذه مكاناً يعتمده ويحيط به من جهاته
One of them is confusion and nonsense from its speaker; that is because what appears in the wax is something like the engraving of the seal, and it is other than it, because the letters present in the wax are part of it and a portion of its parts; and what is in the stamp of letters is part of the stamp and from its entirety, and they are two different things, the existence of each is valid without the other. So their assumption that the very engraving that is in the wax is the same as the stamp is ignorance and confusion. Therefore, if the Word itself is not what appears in the body of Christ, then what appears in it must be other than it and something like it, and that God, exalted, would have two sons and two words: one of them does not indwell bodies nor take them as a temple and place, and the other is indwelling in the body of Christ. This is a statement of four hypostases and abandonment of the doctrine of trinity. As for one who says that the union is only the indwelling of the Word in that with which it is united, its mixing with it and its blending with it, it is said to him: If it is permissible for the Word to indwell in the created body, to blend with it and to mix with it, while it is eternal, then why do you deny its joining with the body and its touching it? And if it is permissible for the Eternal, exalted, to touch, be adjacent, mix with the originated, and blend with it, then why is it not permissible for Him to be opposite the originated and alongside it? And why is it not permissible for Him to have appearance, concealment, movement, distance, proximity, occupation, emptying, formation, and composition? If they seek a distinction in that, they will not find it, and if they pass over that, it is said to them: If it is permissible for that which has this description to be eternal, while in pre-eternity it was not touching, not blending, not mixing, then why do you deny that all other bodies that are touching, mixing, moving, and still could be eternal? And what makes the Word, which has this description, more deserving of eternity than of origination? And what makes bodies more deserving of origination than the Word? And it is said to the Jacobites: If it is permissible for that which is not flesh and blood in itself and which is contrary to flesh and blood in itself to be transformed into flesh and blood through union, then why is it not permissible for the Word, which is contrary to originated things in itself and is not originated in itself, to become originated through union, so that the Eternal in itself becomes originated through union at the time of its union, just as it became flesh and blood at the time of its union? And why does the originated in itself before the union of the Eternal with it not become eternal at the union of the Eternal with it, with which it is united, so that the two natures become one, and that which is not flesh and blood becomes flesh and blood, and that which is flesh and blood becomes not flesh and blood? They will find no way to repel that. It thus departs from being flesh and blood at its union in. As for the statement of one who says that the union is the indwelling of the Word in the humanity without touching, and that it is like the indwelling of the Creator, exalted, in heaven and like His being established on the Throne without touching them, it is false and unintelligible; that is because the Creator, exalted, is not in heaven, nor is He established on His Throne in the sense of His indwelling on the Throne; for if He were indwelling in one of them and established on the other, in the sense of indwelling, it would necessarily entail that He touches them without exception. And as for their statement that the intellect is a substance indwelling in the soul and touching it, it is false because a substance does not indwell in an accident; rather, it indwells in a body in the sense of touching it, depending on it, and taking it as a place on which it depends and which surrounds it from its directions.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a series of refutations of Christian doctrines concerning the incarnation and union (ittihad) of the divine Word with the body of Christ. Baqillani begins by criticizing the analogy of a seal impression on wax. He argues that the letters in wax are part of the wax, not the seal itself; similarly, if the Word (al-kalima) is not identical to what appears in Christ, then there would be two sons and two words, leading to four hypostases instead of the Trinity. Next, he addresses those who claim that union is a mixture (mumazaja) or solution (hulul) of the Word in the body. If the eternal Word can mix with a created body, then why deny physical contact? This would imply that God can be touched, move, etc., which are impossible for the Eternal. He then argues that if something eternal can later become touching and mixing, then other bodies that are always touching could also be eternal, which is absurd. He then turns to the Jacobite doctrine of transformation: if a non-flesh substance can become flesh through union, then the Word could become originated, leading to the absurdity that the eternal becomes temporal. Finally, he refutes the analogy of God's 'indwelling' in heaven or on the Throne without contact. Baqillani asserts that God is not literally in heaven or on the Throne in a spatial sense; if He were, He would have to touch them. He also refutes the idea that the intellect is a substance indwelling in the soul with contact, because a substance cannot indwell in an accident; it indwells only in bodies with contact and dependence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seal impression analogy correctly illustrates the incarnation without reducing the divine to the human.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the impression is part of the wax, not the seal, leading to a multiplication of divine entities if applied to Christ.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what does the seal analogy incorrectly assume?
A) The imprint is the same as the seal.
B) The wax is the same as the seal.
C) The seal has letters.
D) The wax is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's refutation shows that logically, the incarnation cannot be like a seal impression without resulting in multiple divine entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that an appealing analogy can mask a logical fallacy. When you explain Allah's attributes to someone, are you relying on analogies that might subtly distort the truth? What will you do to ensure your explanations are logically sound?
?
Baqillani shows that a small error in reasoning (confusing the imprint with the seal) leads to a great error (multiple divine entities). In your own faith, where might a seemingly harmless assumption about Allah's nature be leading you away from tawheed?
?
The Christians' analogy fails because it treats a created effect (imprint) as if it were the uncreated cause (seal). Are you ever tempted to equate your own understanding or experience of Allah with His actual essence? How can you guard against this confusion?
